Abdel-Maksoud M, Sazonov V, Gutkin SW, Hokanson JE. Effects of modifying triglycerides and triglyceride-rich lipoproteins on cardiovascular outcomes. J Cardiovasc Pharmacol. 2008;51(4):331-351.

## 42 ANSWER: D) Move his insulin glulisine dose to 15 minutes before meals

42 $ ^{A} $ This patient's mean glucose concentration of 131 mg/dL (7.27 mmol/L) using measurements tested before meals and at bedtime is lower than one would expect on the basis of his hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level. A hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ reading of 7.5% (0.075) equates to a mean blood glucose concentration of about 165 mg/dL (9.16 mmol/L). This supports the idea that hyperglycemia is occurring at times when glucose levels are not tested, namely in the postprandial state. Continuous glucose monitoring has demonstrated that unrecognized postprandial hyperglycemia is common in patients with type 1 diabetes and that it contributes adversely to overall glycemic control as measured by hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $.

Postprandial hyperglycemia can be confirmed by having patients test fingerstick glucose levels after meals. Peak glucose levels occur 1 to 2 hours after meals, depending on meal composition. Foods with a higher glycemic index (low in fiber and/or higher in simple sugars) lead to a more rapid and higher peak glucose level than do foods with a lower glycemic index (thus, Answer E is incorrect). The maximum insulin level after injection of one of the rapid-acting insulin analogues occurs about 90 minutes after injection. Reduced postprandial glucose excursions are seen if the insulin injection is moved from immediately before the meal to 15 minutes before the meal (thus, Answer D is correct).

The insulin sensitivity factor can be calculated using the following equation: 1800/total daily insulin dose. In this case, with the patient using about 40 units of insulin daily, the calculated sensitivity factor would be 1:45, or 1 unit to drop the glucose concentration 45 mg/dL (2.50 mmol/L). In this vignette, increasing the patient's sensitivity factor to 1:30 (Answer C) could lead to increased hypoglycemia.

Two-hour postmeal glucose levels are a better predictor of cardiovascular events and mortality than fasting glucose levels, and they are a better predictor of retinopathy in type 2 diabetes mellitus than hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $. Postprandial hyperglycemia increases oxidative stress and is associated with endothelial dysfunction in patients with type 2 diabetes. Compared with regular insulin, rapid-acting insulin analogues lead to better postprandial glycemic control in patients with type 1 diabetes and has been shown to improve cardiovascular risk markers. However, randomized prospective trials targeting postprandial hyperglycemia have not shown reductions in cardiovascular outcomes in patients with type 2 diabetes and have not been performed in patients with type 1 diabetes.

Interventions that lower postprandial glucose responses to a meal include use of rapid-acting insulin analogues rather than human regular insulin, the consumption of lower-glycemic index carbohydrates, warming the injection site before an insulin bolus, the use of continuous glucose monitors, and bolusing insulin 15 minutes before meals rather than immediately before. The addition of protein to a meal increases the postmeal glucose response and the late postmeal insulin requirement. Improvements in postprandial glucose levels can be documented by having a patient check postmeal glucose levels before and after making the change in timing of the premeal rapid-acting insulin dose.

Prandial insulin coverage can effectively control postprandial glucose levels either with set insulin doses within fixed carbohydrate amounts at mealtimes or by having patients learn to count the grams of carbohydrates in a meal and adjust the prandial insulin dose based on a calculated insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio. A formula commonly used to calculate an individual patient's insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio is 500/total daily insulin dose. For this patient, 500/40 units daily results in an insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio of about 1:12 (thus, Answer B is incorrect). Switching him to an insulin-to-carbohydrate ratio would also not be expected to significantly lower postmeal glucose levels because he is eating a set amount of carbohydrates at mealtimes to match his fixed prandial insulin dose. His standard deviation of glucose values is below 50 mg/dL (2.78 mmol/L), indicating a low degree of glucose variability. Higher standard deviations often are seen in patients whose prandial insulin dose does not closely match their carbohydrate intake.

Increasing the insulin glargine dose (Answer A) is a common consideration for patients whose glucose levels are elevated throughout the day and when the glucose level consistently rises overnight while a patient is fasting. In this patient, glucose levels are stable overnight and he periodically experiences nocturnal hypoglycemia. Increasing his insulin glargine dose may increase his risk of nocturnal hypoglycemia.

Educational Objective: Recognize and manage postprandial hyperglycemia in a patient with type 1 diabetes mellitus.

Luijf YM, van Bon AC, Hoekstra JB, Devries JH. Premeal injection of rapid-acting insulin reduces postprandial glycemic excursions in type 1 diabetes. Diarr. Care. 2010;33(10):2152-2155.

43 ANSWER: B) Administer radioiodine with concurrent control with the rate ofனхey disease. This patient has developed symptoms and signs of Graves' ophthalmopathy, an inflammatory eye disease. In most cases, it occurs in patients that develop in the orbit in association with autoimmune thyroid disorders. In most cases, it occurs in patients with current or previous Graves' disease and may present during, before, or after presentation with thyrotoxicosis. Approximately half of patients with Graves' disease have symptoms and signs of ophthalmopathy and 5% have severe disease, sometimes sight-threatening. The natural history of the disease is one of rapid deterioration followed by gradual improvement towards the baseline. Graves' ophthalmopathy is assessed by evaluation of disease activity and severity. The activity is determined using the clinical activity score, which generates 1 point each of the following: pain in primary gaze; pain with eye movement; chemosis; eyelid swelling; eyelid erythema; conjunctival redness; caruncula swelling; and, over the past 3 months, decreased visual acuity, increased diplopia, and proptosis. The maximum score is 10. Graves' ophthalmopathy is considered "active" with a clinical activity score of 3 or higher. The severity is graded as mild, moderate-to-severe, and sight-threatening as agreed upon by a consensus statement of the European Group of Graves' Orbitopathy (EUGOGO). Both activity and severity must be considered in therapeutic decisions regarding treatment of eye disease itself, as well as the treatment of hyperthyroidism. On the basis of the provided information, this patient has mild active Graves' ophthalmopathy.

She is a current smoker, which is the most important effect of smoking on Graves' ophthalmopathy, and several studies have shown a detrimental effect of smoking on Graves' ophthalmopathy in patients treated with radioactive iodine. The risk is directly proportional to the number of cigarettes smoked per day. Former smokers have significantly lower risk than current smokers. Clinicians should advise all patients, and especially those with Graves' ophthalmopathy, to stop smoking and should refer them to a structured smoking-cessation program. Besides smoking, risk factors for the deterioration of Graves' ophthalmopathy after radioiodine therapy include high pretreatment T₃ values, active and progressive Graves' ophthalmopathy over the preceding 3 months, high pretreatment TSH-receptor antibodies, and development of pyothyroidism after treatment. Current American Thyroid Association guidelines recommend steroid prophylaxis patients with mild active Graves' ophthalmopathy. Typical steroid regimens involve a high dosage of oral sucocorticoids for 2 months, although a lower dosage given for 6 weeks could be equally effective.

This patient has developed adverse effects of antithyroid drug treatment and specifically requests a particular acceptable form of treatment for her condition. After discussion of the logistics, benefits, and drawbacks, administration of radioiodine with concurrent glucocorticoid therapy (Answer B) is a reasonable approach. reasons described above, she should be advised to stop smoking, although radioiodine is not contraindicated. light of her smoking status. Radiation protection guidance is variable across the globe, but most national and international protection rules advise against prolonged and close contact with children, especially those aged less than 5 years.

Adverse effects of antithyroid drugs occur in roughly 3% of treated patients and most are minor and transient for adverse effects are rare and include agranulocytosis induced by both methimazole and propylthiouracil, etogenic effects from methimazole, and toxic hepatitis from propylthiouracil. Persistent minor adverse effects of antithyroid drugs should be managed by cessation of medication and recommending radioactive iodine or gery or switching to the other antithyroid drug when radioiodine or surgery is not an option. Replacement methimazole with propylthiouracil (Answer A) is a reasonable strategy in this case, although in view of the need for the treatment (an appropriate therapeutic option in the situation), preference should be given to this treatment strategy.

Although corticosteroid therapy may reduce this patient's symptoms of Graves ophthalmopathy, it is not a long-term solution and only with cessation of the methimazole will the intolerance subside (Answer C is incorrect).

Total thyroidectomy (Answer D) is incorrect because she is requesting radioactive iodine therapy and this is not contraindicated in the current situation.

Because she has had persistent adverse effects from methimazole for 2 months, it is inappropriate to encourage her to continue to take this treatment (Answer E).

## 44 ANSWER: C) Clomiphene citrate Women with polycystic ovary syndrome

Women with polycystic ovary syndrome have infertility related to irregular menstrual cycles and anovulation. Clomiphene citrate (Answer C) is the best first-line medication to induce ovulation in this setting. Clomiphene citrate is a selective estrogen receptor modulator that inhibits estradiol's negative feedback at the hypothalamus, resulting in increased GnRH pulse frequency. The increased GnRH pulsatility stimulates LH and FSH, which then drive follicle growth and estradiol production. The improvement in pregnancy and live birth rate is significant when compared with outcomes using placebo.

Metformin (Answer B) is used off-label for polycystic ovary syndrome because it increases menstrual cycle frequency and pregnancy rate. However, the pregnancy rate is lower than with clomiphene citrate, and metformin does not improve the live birth rate. Therefore, it is not the treatment of choice for infertility. Further, the pregnancy rate using a combination of clomiphene citrate and metformin (Answer D) is not superior to clomiphene alone. In women who do not respond to treatment with clomiphene citrate, recombinant human FSH (Answer A) is the second-line treatment choice.

A recent meta-analysis demonstrated that letrozole, an aromatase inhibitor (Answer E), improves the pregnancy and live birth rate in women with polycystic ovary syndrome when compared with rates observed with clomiphene citrate. However, the quality of the evidence was deemed to be low and it was suggested that these results be regarded with caution. Therefore, an aromatase inhibitor is not yet the recommended treatment for ovulation induction in women with polycystic ovary syndrome until stronger evidence is available.

##### Educational Objective:

Select the most appropriate treatment for a woman with polycystic ovary syndrome who wants to become pregnant.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Treatment of polycystic ovary syndrome in adults

Legro RS, Arslanian SA, Ehrmann DA, et al; Endocrine Society. Diagnosis and treatment of polycystic ovary syndrome: an Endocrine Society Clinical Practice Guideline. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2013;98(12):4565-4592.

Tang T, Lord JM, Norman RJ, Yasmin E, Balen AH. Insulin-sensitising drugs (metformin, rosiglitazone, pioglitazone, D-chiro-inositol) for women with polycystic ovary syndrome, oligo amenorrhoea and subfertility. Cochrane Database Syst Rev. 2012;5:CD003053.

Franik S, Kremer JA, Nelen WL, Farquhar C. Aromatase inhibitors for subfertile women with polycystic ovary syndrome. Cochrane Database Syst Rev. 2014;2:CD010287.

## 45 ANSWER: E) Measure 24-hour urinary calcium and creatinine excretion PTH-depr

45 ANSWER: E) Meas.

PTH-dependent hypercalcemia was correctly diagnosed in this patient. The problem is that the evaluation was incomplete. Although most patients with this pattern of laboratory results have primary hyperparathyroidism, other diagnoses can present with similar biochemistry. Of note, the PTH level in this patient was in the “normal” range. While inappropriately normal PTH levels are not atypical in patients with primary hyperparathyroidism, most have frankly elevated PTH levels. In addition, this patient had no previously documented calcium values for comparison, so it was not possible to distinguish an acquired problem from one that is congenital. Evaluation of such patients should include measurement of 24-hour urinary calcium excretion (Answer E), not so much to determine whether a patient is an appropriate surgical candidate, but rather to exclude familial hypocalciuria, hypercalcemia (FHH). Indeed, this patient’s 24-hour urinary calcium excretion was very low and the fractional excretion of calcium (calcium-to-creatinine ratio) was less than 0.01.

Calculating a calcium-to-creatinine clearance ratio is helpful to distinguish FHH from primary hyperparathyroidism, as 80% of patients with FHH have a ratio less than 0.01 and most patients with primary hyperparathyroidism have a ratio greater than 0.02. The calcium-to-creatinine clearance is calculated with the following equation:  $ [24\ h\ urinary\ calcium \times serum\ creatinine]/[serum\ calcium \times 24\ h\ urinary\ creatinine] $.

FHH is an autosomal dominant disorder with high penetrance and is caused by inactivating mutations in the CASR gene. More than 200 CASR mutations have been described. In this disorder, the parathyroid glands have reduced sensitivity to calcium and higher serum calcium levels are needed to reduce parathyroid release. The calcium-PTH curve is shifted to the left, resetting the serum calcium to a higher level. In the kidney, this produces an increase in tubular calcium and magnesium reabsorption. Typical biochemical abnormalities include hypercalcemia, hypocalciuria, normal or slightly elevated PTH, and elevated magnesium. Genetic testing in the patient revealed that he was heterozygous for a novel mutation in the CASR gene. Persons who are heterozygous for an inactivating CASR mutation typically have mild hypercalcemia due to only partial loss of calcium-sensing receptor function. Although genetic testing might be considered, additional genetic causes unrelated to the CASR gene (eg, the AP2S1 gene) can be responsible for the clinical picture of FHH. Thus, CASR genetic testing is reserved for situations when the patient makes this request or when the urinary fractional excretion of calcium borderline (eg, ~0.01).

Although further imaging of the neck with ultrasonography (Answer A) or 4D CT (Answer B) would be indicated if the patient had persistent primary hyperparathyroidism despite attempted surgical resection, visualization of the parathyroid glands is not indicated in the setting of FHH. Indeed, resection of all 4 glands would result in severe hypocalcemia and thereby cause harm. Most patients with FHH are asymptomatic; do not develop nephrolithiasis due to the low urinary calcium excretion; and do not experience complications such as bone loss, hypertension, or peptic ulcer disease. Neither bone densitometry (Answer D) nor surgery (Answer C) is indicated.

##### Educational Objective:

Include familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia in the differential diagnosis for persistent hyperparathyroidism a parathyroid surgery.

Brown EM. The calcium-sensing receptor: physiology, pathophysiology and CaR-based therapeutics. Subcell Biochem. 2007;45:139-167.

## 46 ANSWER: E) Free testosterone measurement

46 Measurement of serum total testosterone by a reliable assay is the best screening test for the diagnosis of male hypogonadism. Documentation of low serum total testosterone levels (measured on at least 2 occasions) accurately identifies androgen deficiency in most patients. However, when alterations in serum sex hormone-binding globulin (SHBG) concentrations are suspected, further diagnostic steps are required to confirm the diagnosis. Obesity is one such condition in which circulating serum concentrations of SHBG are reduced. Insulin resistance (resulting in hyperinsulinemia) is posited to be the mechanism, as insulin suppresses SHBG production from the liver. Laboratory studies have confirmed that the addition of insulin in the culture medium containing human hepatoma cell line suppresses SHBG production from these cells (Figure). Indeed, low serum total testosterone levels are commonly seen in obese men. Therefore, further diagnostic steps are required to confirm the diagnosis of testosterone deficiency. The next step in these men is the measurement of free testosterone by accurate and reliable methods. These methods include calculation of free testosterone by mass action equation (using SHBG and total testosterone [measured by a reliable assay]) and direct measurement of free testosterone using the equilibrium dialysis method. The former is more often used because it is readily available through commercial laboratories. Although some laboratories have started performing high-throughput assays using equilibrium dialysis, it is still not widely available to all clinicians.

This patient is obese with signs of insulin resistance and his SHBG levels are most likely low. Furthermore, his libido (a specific symptom of androgen deficiency) is normal and his fatigue and drowsiness (nonspecific for androgen deficiency) could be indicative of another underlying condition such as obstructive sleep apnea. Therefore, measurement of free testosterone (Answer E) is the next step in this patient's evaluation. This patient's SHBG levels were documented to be low at 0.79 μg/mL (7.0 nmol/L) and his calculated free testosterone was normal at 12 ng/dL (0.42 nmol/L). Polysomnography confirmed sleep apnea and treatment with continuous positive airway pressure improved his symptoms.

Measurement of gonadotropins (Answer D) is an important aspect in the evaluation of a patient with suspected hypogonadism because gonadotropins indicate whether hypogonadism is primary or secondary. However, they should only be measured after the diagnosis of androgen deficiency is confirmed, which is not the case in this patient. In other words, their measurement in this patient would have been indicated only if his free testosterone were low. Similarly, pituitary MRI (Answer A) and serum prolactin levels (Answer C) should be pursued only after the diagnosis of androgen deficiency is confirmed. Iron studies (Answer B) are indicated if hemochromatosis is suspected, a

condition that can cause either primary or secondary hypogonadism (the latter being more common). The possibility hemochromatosis should only be entertained once the diagnosis of androgen deficiency is confirmed.

##### Educational Objective:

Order appropriate diagnostic tests to confirm the diagnosis of androgen deficiency in obese men.

47 ANSWER: E) The petrosal sinus sampling study

This patient has clear clinical and biochemical evidence of Cushing syndrome and a high ACTH level, supporting a diagnosis of ACTH-dependent Cushing syndrome. The next step in the evaluation is to distinguish between ACTH-dependent Cushing syndrome (Cushing disease) due to an ACTH-secreting pituitary tumor vs an ectopic source of ACTH hypersecretion. This patient underwent dexamethasone suppression testing to help differentiate among the causes. This patient failed to suppress serum cortisol (50%). Suppression of either cortisol levels is a common condition that is not well-classified by the patient. The patient is also a patient who has been农家-skilled with other health-related conditions, such as the use of anticoagulants, and other medical methods.

Petrosal sinus sampling is susceptible to a number of pitfalls. Importantly, for accurate results, hypercortisolemia must be present when inferior petrosal sinus sampling is done. Hypercortisolism in ectopic ACTH syndrome suppresses normal pituitary corticotrophs; thus, administration of corticotropin-releasing hormone does not stimulate ACTH secretion during the sampling procedure. In the presence of eucortisolemia, however, endogenous ACTH production from normal pituitary corticotrophs may no longer be suppressed and stimulation can occur. In this patient, ketoconazole therapy had normalized his cortisol excess, which therefore led to a false-positive result after corticotropin-releasing hormone stimulation (Answer E). The lack of central-peripheral gradient at baseline sampling might be suspicious in this patient, but the stimulation after corticotropin-releasing hormone would be the criterion used to determine whether a positive central-to-peripheral stimulation exists. Guidelines for interpretation of the ACTH gradient obtained from inferior petrosal sinus sampling state that a central-to-peripheral gradient greater than 2.0 at baseline or greater than 3.0 after corticotropin-releasing hormone stimulation is consistent with a pituitary source of Cushing disease. Were the inferior petrosal sinus sampling to have been done properly without pretreatment with ketoconazole, the results would have yielded the little or no central-to-peripheral ACTH gradient, consistent with ectopic ACTH syndrome.

Failure to fully cannulate petrosal sinus vessels (Answer B) can also be a pitfall in test interpretation. Result may fail to show a central-to-peripheral ACTH gradient despite the presence of an ACTH-producing pituitary

tumor if a catheter was not fully advanced into the petrosal sinus. If blood was sampled from a different vein where ACTH hypersecretion was diluted, false-negative results could be obtained. In this patient, a central-to-peripheral gradient was seen after stimulation with corticotropin-releasing hormone, proving the catheters were in place, although the lack of a gradient at time “0” relates to the ectopic source of the ACTH.

Because the patient has a high ACTH level, he clearly does not have ACTH-independent Cushing syndrome from an adrenal source (Answer A). Although patients with pseudo-Cushing syndrome (Answer C) have a central-to-peripheral ACTH gradient on petrosal sinus sampling, as do healthy persons, this patient has clear clinical evidence of Cushing syndrome. Therefore, pseudo-Cushing syndrome is unlikely. A false-positive central-to-peripheral gradient is more likely in this case than a tumor in the cavernous sinus (Answer D). An ectopic corticotropin-releasing hormone-producing pituitary tumor can be associated with false-positive stimulation of ACTH after corticotropin-releasing hormone administration. However, such tumors are extremely rare. Another cause of false-positive central-to-peripheral gradient is intermittent hormonogenesis from an ectopic ACTH source.

##### Educational Objective:

Identify pitfalls of inferior petrosal sinus sampling for the differential diagnosis of Cushing syndrome.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Establishing the cause of Cushing's syndrome

48 ANSWER: C) Change to U500 regular insulin, 0.30 mL (150 units) 3 times daily before meals

This patient has very poor glycemic control despite use of 450 units of U100 insulin daily. He has severe insulin resistance, generally defined as an insulin requirement greater than 200 units of insulin daily. Use of such very high insulin doses is inconvenient because multiple injections per dose are required and high volumes may lead to injection site discomfort. In addition, the absorption of insulin from large-volume subcutaneous depots may be poor and/or unpredictable. In patients such as this, conversion from U100 to 5-fold concentrated U500 regular insulin (U500R) represents a very reasonable alternative glycemic management strategy. In particular, conversion to U500R permits administration of large insulin doses via much smaller volumes per injection and fewer injections per day, which in turn may improve insulin absorption and perhaps regimen adherence. Studies of U500R insulin action suggest that this insulin formulation has an onset of action similar to that of U100 regular insulin and a duration of action similar to that of U100 NPH insulin. Small studies of patients with uncontrolled type 2 diabetes who switch from U100 to U500R insulin have demonstrated improvements in hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level ranging from 1.0% to 3.29%, without a significant increase in rates of severe hypoglycemia. In addition, conversion to U500R in such patients has been associated with improvements in patient satisfaction with treatment and quality of life. Although U500R insulin vials are more expensive than U100 insulin vials, this does not necessarily increase the overall costs of care. The per-unit cost of U500R is estimated to be less than that of U100 insulin, and the costs of needles and syringes may also be reduced because fewer injections are needed.

Although no significant increase in severe hypoglycemia has been reported in clinical trials, use of U500R in clinical practice has resulted in instances of clinically significant hypoglycemia because of dosing errors. Presently, U500R is available only in a vial, and thus must be drawn up and administered via a syringe. Some prescribers prefer to have patients draw up U500R in a U100 insulin syringe with guidance to adapt these devices for the more concentrated insulin. However, the Institute for Safe Medication Practices has recommended “consistent use of a

tuberculin syringe with U-500 insulin, with total doses expressed in terms of both units and volume, i.e., 200 units (0.4 mL). This recommendation has been reiterated in a recent publication dated October 2013. Irrespective of type of syringe chosen for insulin administration, the patient and any associated caregivers must be fully educated regarding the correct drawing up and administration of prescribed doses. Very careful attention must be paid to insulin dosing when users of U500R are hospitalized, as many reported errors in insulin dosing have occurred in hospitalized patients. Guidelines to enhance the safety of insulin use during hospitalizations have also been issued by the Institute for Safe Medication Practices. Delivery devices to administer concentrated insulin more safely currently under development.

Recommendations for the conversion from U100 insulin to U500R generally incorporate the baseline U100 insulin doses, as well as hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level, to determine appropriate doses of the concentrated insulin. In general, experts recommend that the total daily insulin dose be increased by 10% in patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level above 10% (>0.100); that the total daily insulin dose be maintained in patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level between 8% and 10% (0.080 and 0.100); and that the total daily dose be decreased by 10% to 20% in patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level below 8% (<0.080) at the time of conversion. It should be noted, however, that some experts recommend total daily insulin dose reductions of 10% to 20% for all patients when their regimen converted to U500R. In patients using 150 to 300 units of insulin daily, the total amount may be administered in 3 divided doses, while users of 300 to 600 units daily require 3 to 4 injections daily or continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion. Users of more than 600 units of insulin daily should have their regimens converted to either 4 insulin injections daily or continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion.

insulin injections daily or continuous subcutaneous insulin inpatient daily or continuous daily or continuous daily or continuous daily or U100 insulin daily and has a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level of 9.1% (0.091). Conversion to U500R at 0.2 mL (100 units) 3 times daily (Answer A) represents a greater than 100 of U500R at 0.45 mL (225 units) twice daily (Answer B) provides an overall appropriate (maintained) total daily dose of insulin; however, users of this much daily insulin should divide the total amount into at least 3 separate injections. Conversion to 0.3 mL (150 units) 3 times daily (Answer C) is correct because the patient's total daily insulin dose is unchanged and divided into an appropriate number of doses. Conversion to U500R dosing of 0.4 mL (200 units) 3 times daily (Answer D) represents a more significant increase than is generally recommended, even for patients with a hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level greater than 10% (>0.100). U500R insulin delivered via continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion is a therapeutic option; however, this is not a requirement for U500R use and it has not been demonstrated to be more effective than the use of U500R via multiple daily injections. Thus, referral for insulin pump training before conversion to U500R (Answer E) would unnecessarily delay implementation of effective therapy and would certainly increase therapeutic costs. Although some data suggest that delivery of U500R via continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion may cause less weight gain than administration via multiple daily injection the 2 delivery strategies have not been adequately compared in randomized controlled trials. Thus, any perceived metabolic advantage to the use of U500R via continuous subcutaneous insulin infusion remains hypothetical.

##### Educational Objective:

Guide conversion from a U100 to a U500 regular insulin regimen in a patient with severe insulin resistance.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Insulin therapy in adults with type 1 diabetes mellitus

Dailey AM, Tannock LR. Extreme insulin resistance: indications and approaches to the use of U-500 insulin in type 2 diabetes mellitus. Curr Diab Rep 2011;11(2):77-82.

Institute for Safe Medication Practices 2013 ISMP Medication Safety Alert. As U-500 Insulin Safety Concerns Mount, It's Time to Rethink Safe Use of Smart Above U-100. Available at www.ismp.org/newsletters/acutecare/showarticle.asp?id=62. Accessed January 18, 2014.

## 40 ANSWER: D) Increase dexamethasone to 0.5 mg at bedtime

In nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia, the adrenal zona fasciculata's production of cortisol is partially reduced such that the hypothalamic-pituitary drive to the adrenal gland is increased to restore normal cortisol levels.

a result, the intermediate steroid metabolites between cholesterol as it is converted to cortisol accumulate. In particular, with disturbances in 21-hydroxylase activity, ACTH secretion rises above normal and 17-hydroxyprogesterone and DHEA-S are increased because they are proximate to the enzymatic defect. DHEA-S is a weak androgen, so elevated DHEA-S levels can inhibit hypothalamic GnRH, pituitary LH, and testicular testosterone secretion.

Nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia is not life-threatening because of compensated production of glucocorticoids. It presents in childhood or early adulthood. In females, elevated adrenal androgen production can manifest with acne, hirsutism, and irregular menses. In males, adrenal androgen production can increase before the time of normal puberty and lead to a premature growth spurt and virilization. Hypertension occurs, albeit infrequently, if intermediate molecules with mineralocorticoid activity are increased.

The central question related to this vignette focuses on glucocorticoid replacement when symptoms from the partial enzymatic defect arise. For this patient, adrenal androgen production initially resulted in premature puberty and later in reduced gonadal function and erectile dysfunction. To address his concerns, longer-acting glucocorticoid medications should be administered at bedtime with a goal of reducing early-morning ACTH secretion and subsequent adrenal hormone synthesis. Generally, 1 mg of dexamethasone taken at bedtime will suppress most, if not all, ACTH secretion in normal individuals. Hence, dexamethasone dosages below 1 mg daily are often sufficient to prevent adrenal androgen hypersecretion in patients with nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia. However, metabolism of dexamethasone varies among individuals, and some patients require more, or less, dexamethasone to experience a satisfactory response without signs and symptoms of glucocorticoid excess.

This patient was clearly undertreated at a dexamethasone dosage of 0.25 mg at bedtime. Thus, an increase to 0.5 mg at bedtime would be a reasonable next step (thus, Answer D is correct). Because of dexamethasone's long duration of action, splitting it into 2 doses is not needed and taking the dexamethasone in the morning would exert a suppressive action on ACTH at a time when it would not be elevated (ie, the afternoon and evening) (thus, Answer E is incorrect).

Generally, the ratio of glucocorticoid action for dexamethasone to hydrocortisone is ~25:1. A dose of 12.5 mg of hydrocortisone is roughly equivalent to 0.5 mg of dexamethasone. However, one would not use hydrocortisone at bedtime to suppress ACTH secretion because of its shorter duration of action (thus, Answer A is incorrect). Similar to splitting the dexamethasone dose, using twice-daily hydrocortisone is also unlikely to be helpful (thus, Answer B is incorrect).

Adding testosterone (Answer C) would increase circulating testosterone levels and might improve the erectile dysfunction. However, adding an additional medication should not be needed if the dexamethasone dosage is properly adjusted.

##### Educational Objective:

Treat nonclassic congenital adrenal hyperplasia and differentiate among glucocorticoid preparations.

Previous attempts to identify the source of the thyroglobulin have failed to document residual or recurrent disease, until this current ultrasound.

## 51 ANSWER: A) Diabetic amyotrophy

Diabetic amyotrophy (Answer A) is also known as Bruns-Garland syndrome. Because of the considerable diabetic neuropathy, diabetic polyradiculopathy, diabetic motor neuropathy, diabetic radiculoplexopathy, diabetic lumbosacral plexopathy, and diabetic lumbosacral radiculoplexus neuropathy. However, diabetic amyotrophy

affects both the lumbosacral nerve roots and peripheral nerves; thus, it is not a pure lumbosacral plexopathy as is the case with idiopathic lumbosacral radiculoplexus neuropathy. Although lumbosacral radiculoplexus neuropathy is high on the differential diagnosis list, it is not associated with diabetes. This, along with the lack of involvement of peripheral nerves, helps distinguish it from diabetic neuropathy.

Diabetic amyotrophy is seen at any stage of diabetes neuropathy. in middle-aged patients with reasonable diabetes control or in those with a recent diabetes diagnosis. Sometimes diabetic amyotrophy can be the presenting feature of diabetes. It is more prevalent in patients with type 2 diabetes or type 1 diabetes. Concomitant weight loss is common. Affected persons usually have unilateral proximal hypoflexia due to the loss of the body'sி nteroverset with the lack of involvement of the type of diabetes in the body.

dyspnea is often found as a subset of patients have no associated pain; weakens; weight loss; and autonomic dysfunction such as orthostatic dysfunction, tachycardia, altered sweating, etc. Upper limb or thoracic involvement may occur. Affected patients may present with asymmetric weakness involving the proximal extremity. However, over time (months), the signs and symptoms progress to the contralateral extremity and become symmetric. Neurophysiologic testing typically shows a predominant axonal neuropathy, and nerve biopsy may document microvasculitis. Neuroimaging can be used to exclude other differential diagnoses. Nerve conduction studies reveal marked reduction in amplitudes of the compound muscle and sensory nerve action potentials, while conduction velocities are not as significant. The diagnosis of diabetic amyotrophy is made predominantly on the basis of a conglomeration of

Treatment includes narcotic analgesic agents, antidepressant medications, and occupational and physical therapy. Immune-modulating therapy or steroid therapy is not helpful. Complete recovery is uncommon, but clinical improvement can be accomplished with antidepressants, physical therapy, patient education, and reassurance.

Vitamin B $ _{12} $ deficiency (Answer B) usually presents with symmetric neuropathy that affects the legs more than the arms. The initial presenting symptom is paresthesias and ataxia associated with loss of vibration and position sense, which may progress to severe weakness, spasticity, clonus, paraplegia, and even fecal and urinary incontinence.

Chronic inflammatory demyelinating polyradiculoneuropathy (Answer C) generally presents with symmetric weakness with various sensory symptoms that exhibit either a relapsing, stepwise progressive, or steadily progressive course. In addition to limb weakness, affected patients may also present with fatigue, numbness, tingling, or tight sensation in their extremities. Pain is rarely a component of chronic inflammatory demyelinating polyradiculoneuropathy. Diminished or absent reflexes on neurologic testing is the rule. Cranial nerve involvement occurs in some cases and loss of micturition control due to involvement of pelvic neural circuitry is relatively common. Meralgia paresthetica (Answer D) is characterized by burning pain, tingling, numbness, and decreased sensation over the lateral thigh, corresponding to the distribution of the lateral femoral cutaneous nerve. This nerve does not contain motor fibers, so affected patients do not have motor deficits such as those observed in the vignette.

Muscle infarct (Answer E) is associated with elevated creatine phosphokinase values and lack of associated neurologic involvement, making it an unlikely diagnosis in this vignette.

##### Educational Objective:

Diagnose diabetic amyotrophy, an important category of lumbosacral plexopathies, on the basis of clinical features.

## 52 ANSWER: E) Continue the current statin at the same dosage

52 The issue in this vignette is the safety of statin use in patients who consume grapefruit (fruit or juice). The lay literature is ripe with danger warnings about the consumption of grapefruit while taking statins, and patients

are routinely counseled to avoid grapefruit or its juice when taking certain medications, including statins. Grapefruit and its juice contain a number of compounds that irreversibly inhibit CYP3A enzymes that are key in the metabolism of a number of drugs. Most statins undergo significant metabolism by the CYP3A system; thus, if the enzymes are inhibited, this leads to increased systemic plasma concentrations of statins, which has the potential to increase clinical and adverse effects. Because the CYP3A system is irreversibly inhibited, this can have effects on drug clearance for the several days after even a single dose. However, the impact of inhibiting CYP3A on statin metabolism is related to the clearance pathway and bioavailability of the specific agent. For example, simvastatin undergoes extensive first-line metabolism by CYP3A4, so its mean oral bioavailability is about 5%; inhibition of CYP3A4 could lead to significant increases—up to 10-fold—in its bioavailability. Conversely, atorvastatin has a higher mean oral bioavailability of 15% to 30%, so concentrations are only modestly increased by CYP3A inhibitors. Another consideration is how the grapefruit or juice does it take to have a significant impact on CYP3A? Several small pharmacokinetic studies suggest that it takes more than 500 mL of grapefruit juice on a daily basis to affect drug metabolism. Consumption of 600 to 750 mL of grapefruit juice each day has been shown to increase the plasma concentration of atorvastatin by 1.3-fold. However, it is quite rare to consume that much grapefruit or grapefruit juice on a regular basis. One study evaluated more commonly consumed amounts: one 300-mL (10-oz) glass of grapefruit juice per day for 90 days in long-term atorvastatin users. Participants were randomly assigned to continue their usual atorvastatin dosage or to decrease their baseline dosage by 50% when starting the grapefruit juice. Participants who continued their baseline dosage had an increase in atorvastatin plasma concentrations of up to 26% compared with concentrations at baseline with negligible changes in lipid levels and no adverse effects on liver function or serum creatine phosphokinase values. Participants randomly assigned to decrease their baseline dosage by 50% had a decrease in plasma atorvastatin concentrations of 12% to 25%, with an increase in LDL-cholesterol concentration (9%) and triglycerides (23%) compared with baseline concentrations. Again, no adverse effects on liver function or creatine phosphokinase levels were observed. Thus, on the basis of this study's findings, the Answer E is correct—no change in statin dosage is needed. The prescribing information raises concern with “excessive grapefruit juice consumption >1.2 liters/day.”

If this patient had any other risk factors such as use of other C. some calcium channel blockers, some macrolide antibiotics), were frail, or were taking multiple medications, then decreasing the statin dosage (Answer B) might be reasonable. Given this patient's high risk for a recurrent cardiovascular event, discontinuing the statin, even if starting an alternative agent such as a fibrate or ezetimibe, would not be appropriate (thus, Answers A, C, D are incorrect).

It is important to note that different statins are subject to different metabolism, and thus the response to grapefruit juice or other inhibitors of CYP3A4 is agent specific. Most studies evaluating the effect of grapefruit� statins are relatively short term (see Table).

## 53 ANSWER: B) Gene expression classifier Thyroid nodules occur in 20% to 75% of ac

53 Thyroid nodules occur in 20% to 75% of adults and 5% to 15% of these are proven to be malignant. FNAB is the most reliable clinical tool for determining whether a nodule is benign or malignant. However, approximately 10% to 25% of FNABs of thyroid nodules yield an indeterminate result. The Bethesda System for reporting thyroid cytopathology is used to classify these lesions as atypia of undetermined significance or follicular lesions of undetermined significance (AUS/FLUS—Bethesda Category III) or follicular neoplasm/suspicious for follicular neoplasm (FN/SFN—Bethesda Category IV). The reported risk of malignancy in these indeterminate lesions varies and is 7% to 48% for AUS/FLUS lesions and 20% to 30% for FN/SFN lesions. The Bethesda classification also labels lesions suspicious for malignancy as category V and the reported malignancy risk in these lesions is 50% to 75%. Recent advances in thyroid cancer biology have led to the development of several tests to assist in determining whether indeterminate nodules are benign or malignant. Currently, molecular tests to refine the diagnosis of thyroid malignancy are meant to complement and not to replace clinical judgment, sonographic assessment, and visual cytopathology interpretation.

Two main tests are currently marketed to assess for malignancy in thyroid nodules: gene expression classifiers (Answer B) and mutation analysis panels (Answer C). Gene expression classifiers are based on gene expression profiles of surgically proven benign and malignant thyroid nodules. They evaluate for the presence of a benign expression profile consisting of 142 mRNAs. Validation studies have shown high negative predictive values (94% to 95%) and sensitivity (90%) among nodules with the cytologic interpretation of AUS/FLUS or FN/SFN. Gene expression classifiers are therefore considered a “rule out” test to identify nodules that are benign. However, because a substantial number of benign nodules do not have a gene expression profile classified as benign, the test can therefore not always exclude malignancy. Moreover, clinical validation has not been performed for nodules smaller than 1 cm, nor has this form of testing been evaluated in the pediatric population. The use of this test has been reported to obviate the need for surgical excision in approximately 50% of cases. The specificity (52%) and positive predictive value (47%) of gene expression classifiers in nodules with indeterminate cytology are poor and their use is therefore not recommended to confirm the presence of malignancy. Preliminary cost analysis indicates that the use of gene expression classifiers may be cost-effective, although studies performed thus far have been largely based on preliminary data. Because the patient in this vignette has a lesion classified as FLUS (which carries a relatively low risk of malignancy), proceeding with a gene expression classifier test (Answer B) would be

the most appropriate of the listed options. The false-negative rate is 5% to 6%, so patients with a negative result should still be followed up closely with repeated ultrasonography.

BRAF Val600Glu and KAS point mutations, as well as common rearrangements of RET/PTC and PAX8/PPARG, are common drivers behind many thyroid cancers. It is estimated that 1 of these molecular parameters is present in 60% of well-differentiated thyroid cancer. The components of the mutation analysis panel are available as standalone tests or together as panels that assess for the presence of these molecular alterations simultaneously. Mutation analysis has high specificity and very good positive predictive value. However, because approximately 40% of malignant nodules with indeterminate cytology do not harbor any of these genetic mutations, they can reliably exclude malignancy (thus, Answers C and E are incorrect). They are considered good “rule-in” tests to confirm the presence of malignancy and may be used in lesions that are suspicious for malignancy. There is presently no consensus for the use of mutation testing as a guide to surgical decision-making, and mutation testing does not have a role in determining the extent of lymph node dissection. However, because the presence of a mutation such as BRAF Val600Glu has a positive predictive value of nearly 100% for the presence of papillary thyroid cancer, this finding might well justify the consideration of total rather than hemithyroidectomy.

The TSH receptor assay uses quantitative reverse transcription–polymerase chain reaction to detect circulating thyroid cancer cells by assessing the serum concentration of thyrotropin receptor mRNA from a standard peripheral blood specimen. This assay may be useful as a marker of thyroid cancer both in the setting of initial diagnosis and disease recurrence as a rule-out test. In lesions with FN/SFN, the addition of high-quality neck ultrasonography to diagnostic algorithm incrementally improved assay sensitivity to 97%. However, this assay has been developed and evaluated specifically in the Cleveland Clinic and the recorded statistics are only applicable if patients also undergo ultrasonography evaluation at the same facility. This assay has achieved less scrutiny in the medical literature and most of the data are based on the single-institutional experience of the Cleveland Clinic (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

Immunocytochemical analysis of proteins including galectin-3 and HBM in a high proportion of papillary carcinomas, has also been evaluated. These tests are generally less expensive than the genetic and genomic tests, although their cost-effectiveness has not been systematically evaluated. This type analysis has a potential role in the field and has better specificity and positive predictive value, albeit less accurate than mutation analysis panels.

##### Educational Objective:

## 54 ANSWER: E) Perform another DXA scan

54 The DXA scan is an essential tool used in the evaluation and follow-up of patients with low bone density and osteoporosis. Measurement of bone density is the best correlate with fracture risk and it is an integral part of the FRAX analysis for long-term risk assessment. However, DXA is not without potential error. Proper and consistent positioning of the patient, in particular for measurement of the femoral neck and hip, is critical. If one examines the 2 sets of images presented in the vignette, a clear difference in technique can be observed. The DXA from 2 years earlier (along with the first and second studies in the sequence) includes some portion of the pelvis in the measurement of the femoral neck (see arrow).

## 55 ANSWER: C) Change to 45 units of once-daily basal, long-acting insulin and 15 units of rapid-acting insulin before meals

This patient with type 2 diabetes mellitus has what appears to be a marked discrepancy between his home capillary plasma glucose readings and his hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ values. Although not specifically addressed in this question, this discordance is sometimes due to conditions such as anemia, hemoglobinopathy, or clinically significant renal dysfunction, which may contribute to unreliable hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ results. In the absence of such a condition, self-monitored blood glucose values much lower than that suggested by the hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ level should prompt an investigation for hyperglycemia occurring at times other than those routinely monitored.

This patient has undergone continuous glucose monitoring over a consecutive 5-day period. As displayed in the accompanying image, the data obtained during this monitoring reveal consistent and significant increases in his glucose levels following the midday and evening meals.

However, this is not the best next step in his care because adequate data are available now to modify the patient's insulin regimen. Modification of this patient's insulin regimen to 1 injection daily of basal, long-acting insulin and rapid-acting insulin doses given at all meals (Answer C) is the step that will most effectively address postprandial control of glucose at all times of day. The insulin doses outlined in this answer (45 units of once-daily basal, long-acting insulin and 15 units of rapid-acting insulin before meals) provide 50% of his total daily insulin dose as basal, and divide the remaining 50% among his 3 meals. Continued and more frequent blood glucose testing is also required to appropriately adjust his insulin doses from this initial regimen, particularly if some also are much larger and/or higher in carbohydrates than others.

Modification of the patient's 75/25 insulin dosing to 60 units before breakfast and 30 units before the evening (Answer D) may have unpredictable results. Although the increased morning dose might to some extent be the rise in glucose following the midday meal, it is unclear whether the peak effect of the morning dose is fixed insulin will coincide closely with the absorption of carbohydrates from that meal. In addition, given the minimal rise in blood glucose after breakfast, it is possible that an increased dose of mixed insulin in the ongoing may result in hypoglycemia before the midday meal. Furthermore, this dosing change does not address hyperglycemia that occurs after the evening meal. Similarly, modification in the patient's 75/25 insulin dosing units before breakfast and 40 units before the evening meal (Answer E) might improve his glycemic control by the evening meal and perhaps overnight as well. However, this dosing change would be unlikely to affect his hyperglycemia whatsoever. This modification might also increase his risk of hypoglycemia overnight or by morning hours.

## 57 ANSWER: D) Add hydrochlorothiazide, 50 mg daily

57 A Hypoparathyroidism due to autosomal dominant hypocalcemia is most commonly caused by a heterozygous activating mutation in the gene encoding the calcium-sensing receptor (CASR). As a result, the second point for calcium sensing is reduced, thereby reducing PTH secretion from the parathyroid gland, which results in hypocalcemia and stimulation of calcuires from the kidneys. This can lead to an increase in nephrolithiasis and nephrocalcinosis and may result in chronic kidney disease. Calcium supplementation and calcitriol are the main stays of therapy. However, urinary calcium excretion may increase in response to vitamin D therapy even before normalization of serum calcium levels. Therefore, the goals of therapy are to maintain serum calcium in the normal range and to avoid hypercalciuria.

Thiazide diuretics reduce urinary calcium excretion via activation of a sodium-calcium transporter and they reduce hypercalciuria associated with hypoparathyroidism. Thus, hydrochlorothiazide (Answer D) can prove both urinary and serum calcium levels.

The effect of hypocholesterolism on the levels of the 25-hydroxyvitamin D is notuஃஞ்ஞு.

may be modest differences in absorption of calcium from calcium citrate compared with absorption from carbonate, but this is of little clinical import. Calcium carbonate contains a greater amount of elemental calcium than calcium citrate and hence fewer milligrams of supplement need to be taken. It should be noted that patient carbonate can have adverse effects including eructation, flatulence, and constipation when compared with associated with calcium citrate. However, because this patient is already on a stable dosage of oral calcium, there is no evidence to suggest that switching to calcium citrate (Answer A) would be beneficial.

## 58 ANSWER: D) Calcium oxalate crystals

Enteric hyperoxaluria and calcium oxalate stone formation are well-recognized complications of malabsorptive bariatric operations such as the jejunoileal bypass. Until recently, the risk for hyperoxaluria and calcium oxalate stone formation with modern bariatric surgery, mainly Roux-en-Y gastric bypass, was unrecognized. Investigators have documented this association and report almost a doubling of the risk for the development of renal stone disease when compared with the risk of obese control participants. However, the overall risk for stone formation appears to be low even in patients with a history of renal stone disease and should not represent a contraindication to bariatric surgery. Ongoing investigation and surveillance is advised to identify patients.

The risk for renal stone disease is highest during the first 5 years after Roux-en-Y gastric bypass. The most common laboratory abnormalities reported include hyperoxaluria (>100 mg/24 h urine specimen), low urinary citrate (<500 mg/24 h urine specimen) (thus, Answer B is incorrect), and low urine volumes (<1.5 L) (thus, Answer E is incorrect). Patients with markedly low urine volumes (<1 L) can have substantial elevations in calcium oxalate supersaturations despite minimal abnormalities in urinary oxalate and citrate excretion. Although obesity is associated with higher risk for uric acid renal stones, the risks drops significantly after bariatric surgery and uric acid excretion is low (thus, Answer C is incorrect). Calcium excretion varies depending on dietary calcium intake and calcium supplementation, but it is rarely elevated (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

Increased calcium oxalate supersaturation was the most common abnormality reported in study examining a patient cohort after Roux-en-Y gastric bypass (thus, Answer D is correct). A proposed mechanism for the observed hyperoxaluria is increased intestinal absorption of oxalate with diets low in calcium and high in fat. As with any renal stone disease, low urine volumes increase the risk. For at-risk patients, recommendations include sufficient fluid intake to maintain a urine volume greater than 2 L daily, restriction of dietary fat, and adequate calcium intake either from dietary sources or supplementation.

##### Educational Objective:

Predict characteristics of the urine supersaturation profile in patients at risk for calcium oxalate renal stones after Roux-en-Y gastric bypass.

Matlaga BR, Shore AD. Magnuson T. Clark JM. Johns R, Makary MA. Effect of gastric bypass surgery on kidney stone disease. J Urol. 2009;181(6):2573-2577.

Park AM, Storm DW, Fulmer BR, Still CD, Wood GC, Hartle JE 2nd. A prospective study of risk factors for nephrolithiasis after Roux-en-Y gastric bypass surgery. J Urol. 2009;182(5):2334-2339.

## 59 ANSWER: D) Review the angiogram with the radiologist

59 ANSWER:

The results of the selective arterial calcium stimulation test cannot be interpreted without knowledge of the arterial anatomy of the pancreas. An angiogram is performed at the start of the procedure to guide catheter placement and is important to the interpretation of the results. In this vignette, the results would, at face value, suggest either the presence of multiple islet tumors or more rare conditions such as noninsulinoma, pancreatogenous hypoglycemia syndrome with diffuse involvement of the pancreas. In approximately 75% of the normal population, the gastroduodenal artery supplies the head of the pancreas, the superior mesenteric artery supplies part of the body, and the splenic artery supplies the remainder of the pancreas, including the tail. This information is usually helpful in regionalizing (but not, strictly speaking, localizing) insulinomas. However, because of the inherent frequency of anomalies in the arterial supply of the pancreas, it is important that the test results be interpreted in light of the arterial anatomy of the individual patient.

In this vignette, the patient's arterial circulation had acquired an abnormality—namely, an atherosclerotic stenosis of the celiac axis and superior mesenteric artery with the development of an extensive collateral network so that injection of calcium into any 1 of the 3 main arterial trunks was accompanied by significant retrograde flow along the collateral circulation. Therefore, the selective arterial calcium stimulation test results are useless in regionalizing her tumor. Consulting with the radiologist (Answer D) is the best next step.

Before the advent of arterial stimulation testing or other invasive studies, intraoperative ultrasonography to localize insulinomas. Although it may be reasonable to recommend surgery (Answer B) on the basis of the available data, there are no data in the clinical vignette to support a distal pancreatectomy since an insulinoma could not be regionalized to the pancreatic tail and could be anywhere in the pancreas. Blind distal pancreatectomy is rarely indicated.

Compared with CT, MRI (Answer C) has inferior resolving power because of the motion artifact associated with breathing, and it is usually not used in the evaluation of insulinoma.

Positron emission tomography (Answer E) is helpful in managing hyperinsulinemic hypoglycemia in infancy but it is far from certain whether this is superior to conventional imaging in the evaluation of hypoglycemic disorders in adults. Fluoro F 18 DOPA has been used in pediatric hypoglycemia, although there are case reports of radiolabeled glucagonlike peptide 1 receptor agonists being used for this purpose. Their role and superiority over conventional imaging are debatable.

## 60 ANSWER: E) Spironolactone and norethindrone-only hormonal contraception

60 The risk of stroke is increased in patients with migraines and visual aura compared with the risk in patients who have migraines without aura. A concerning visual aura includes a zigzag line that moves laterally in the visual field or an area of lost vision that spreads laterally surrounded by normal visual fields, as described in the vignette. The etiology of a visual aura may be ischemia, which could underlie the increased risk for stroke. Studies suggest that the risk of stroke is 2 to 4 times higher for women with migraines taking oral contraceptives than those without, but no studies have had sufficient numbers to examine women with aura vs those without aura. The risk of stroke is so low in women of reproductive age that migraines without aura are not a contraindication to hormonal contraception as long as a woman is healthy, younger than 35 years, and does not smoke. In contrast, migraines with aura at any age are a contraindication for combined estrogen and progestin hormonal contraception (Answer D).

In this vignette, the patient wants treatment for hirsutism and acne. While combined estrogen and progestin is the treatment of choice for acne and hirsutism, the therapy is contraindicated in this patient. Spironolactone and norethindrone-only contraception (Answer E) would be the best combination to treat her primary symptoms.

Spironolactone, used off-label for polycystic ovary syndrome, alleviates acne and hirsutism through blockade of androgen receptor in the skin. Progestin-only contraception, which does not exacerbate migraines, protects the uterus from unopposed endogenous estrogen exposure. It also prevents pregnancy, which is important when spironolactone is used because it causes ambiguous genitalia in male fetuses in animal models and is classified as a pregnancy category C drug.

Although progestin-only contraception in the form of a subcutaneous depot (medroxyprogesterone), pill (norethindrone), or coated intrauterine device (levonorgestrel) (Answer C) would all be acceptable alternatives for contraception and uterine protection, they do not address her primary concern when used alone. Similarly, monthly micronized progesterone (Answer A) protects the uterus from unopposed estrogen exposure, but does not address the cosmetic concerns or the possibility of pregnancy. Metformin (Answer B) can increase the number of ovulatory menstrual cycles in women with polycystic ovary syndrome, but it is not a good treatment for hirsutism or acne.

##### Educational Objective:

## 61 ANSWER: A) Switch to a non-opiate-containing analgesic

This is a case of hypogonadotropic hypogonadism. Given the patient's age, previous fertility, and evidence of normal secondary sexual characteristics, this is not a congenital problem. Acquired secondary hypogonadism is most often due to structural disease of the pituitary gland, hyperprolactinemia, or exogenous drug use.

In this case, hypogonadotropic hypogonadism developed because of chronic opiate use. This patient was consuming 100 mg of morphine daily. When opiates are administered on a long-term basis, especially when they are administered continuously for relief of chronic pain, they often cause pronounced secondary hypogonadism. This has been most often reported in association with high-dosage methadone use, but any opiate used for more than a few weeks at a dosage in excess of the equivalent of 100 mg of oral morphine daily can be associated with secondary hypogonadism. The mechanism is not entirely clear, but is thought to be due to inhibition of hypothalamic GnRH production by morphine. Additionally, studies have shown that opiate administration can reduce activity of 5α-reductase and thus reduce peripheral testosterone production.

Although not always practical, the optimal management strategy is to attempt to restore pituitary-gonadal function by gradual withdrawal of opiates and replacement with non-opiate-containing analgesics (Answer A). Only if this strategy fails or is not practical would consideration of testosterone replacement be appropriate. It is also important to note that long-term opiate use can also impair adrenal function via inhibition of corticotropin-releasing hormone and ACTH production.

Although sildenafil (Answer B) is an effective treatment of erectile dysfunction, it is not appropriate as a first-line strategy in this case because the underlying cause should be addressed first.

Chronic opiate use can also elevate prolactin levels, but it does not cause prolactinoma. This patient's serum prolactin concentration is mildly elevated, but this is consistent with opiate use and, crucially, is not high enough that it would be expected to be associated with secondary hypogonadism. Withdrawal of opiates should lower this patient's serum prolactin. Therefore, use of dopamine agonists such as cabergoline (Answer C) is not required. Associated with erectile dysfunction, but not with

Hypertension per se and antihypertensive drugs can be associated with hypogonadism. Although adjustment of antihypertensive therapy could be considered, this should only be done after addressing any underlying causes of erectile dysfunction. Moreover, of all antihypertensive drugs, thiazide diuretics (Answer E) and  $ \beta $-adrenergic blockers (Answer D) are associated with a higher incidence of erectile dysfunction than either ACE inhibitors or calcium channel blockers and would not be appropriate substitutes for these antihypertensive drugs.

Describe the effects of long-term opiate use on pituitary function.

## 62 ANSWER: B) Discontinue the oral contraceptive

62 ANSWER: B) Discontinue the oral contraceptive. The assessment of elevated steroid concentrations must always account for the presence of factors (including medications) that may increase the levels of steroid-binding proteins, conditions that alter metabolic clearance of steroids, or medications that may directly affect steroid-receptor binding. This young woman is taking an oral contraceptive containing drospirenone and ethinyl estradiol. Estrogen-containing oral contraceptives are well known to cause marked increases in the major binding protein for cortisol (corticosteroid-binding globulin). Women taking oral contraceptives usually have basal total cortisol levels that exceed 20 μg/dL (552 nmol/L) and more than 50% have an abnormal result from an overnight 1-mg dexamethasone suppression test. Although it is commonly thought that oral contraceptives do not affect urinary free cortisol excretion, a recent study showed that nearly one-third of women taking oral contraceptives had a mild elevation of urinary free cortisol. In contrast, late-night salivary cortisol (a reflection of biologically active free cortisol) is usually normal in these women. Consequently, this woman has no biochemical or clinical evidence of hypercortisolism, and the use of a glucocorticoid receptor antagonist such as mifepristone (Answer D) or a drug to inhibit adrenal steroidogenesis such as ketoconazole (Answer E) is not indicated. In contrast to cortisol, aldosterone has weak binding to corticosteroid-binding globulin, and estrogens do not significantly alter its concentration.

Drospirenone is a unique progestin that is actually an analogue of the antimineralocorticoid receptor antagonist spironolactone. The antimineralocorticoid activity of drospirenone made it popular because it tends to be associated with less fluid retention in young women; however, drospirenone has also been associated with an increased risk for thromboembolic disease, which has limited its use. Similar to spironolactone, drospirenone causes marked increases in plasma and urinary aldosterone because of the mineralocorticoid receptor blockade. Accordingly, plasma renin activity also increases. Therefore, the hyperaldosteronism in this patient is related to the antimineralocorticoid activity of drospirenone. Discontinuation of the oral contraceptive (Answer B) normalized her blood pressure. In a prospective cohort study in the United States of nearly 70,000 normotensive, female

nurses aged 25 to 42 years, the age-adjusted relative risk of hypertension when using oral contraceptives was 1.5 (95% confidence interval, 1.2-1.8) for current use and 1.1 (95% confidence interval, 0.9-1.2) for past use. After adjustment for age, body mass index, cigarette smoking, family history of hypertension, physical activity, alcohol intake, and ethnicity, current users of oral contraceptives had an increased risk for development of hypertension (relative risk, 1.8; 95% confidence interval, 1.5-2.3) compared with the risk for women who had never used oral contraceptives. The mechanism of how oral contraceptives increase blood pressure is unknown.

The vignette contains 2 other clues that this woman does not have true primary aldosteronism: (1) the nonsuppressed plasma renin activity and (2) the absence of hypokalemia with prodigious elevations of aldosterone. In patients with primary aldosteronism, the plasma renin activity is almost always less than 1 ng/mL per hour if the patient is not receiving any medications that alter the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system. Even patients with primary aldosteronism who are taking an ACE inhibitor or an angiotensin-receptor antagonist rarely have a plasma renin activity greater than 1 ng/mL per hour. Hypokalemia would be inevitable in a patient with aldosterone concentrations of this magnitude in the absence of a mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist. Although this woman has a markedly elevated aldosterone-to-plasma renin activity ratio (simply because the numerator is falsely elevated), the nonsuppressed plasma renin activity excludes primary aldosteronism as a reasonable diagnosis. Therefore, a mineralocorticoid receptor antagonist such as spironolactone (Answer A) is not warranted.

Licorice-induced hypermineralocorticoidism is a well-appreciated cause of hypertension and hypokalemic metabolic alkalosis. Licorice contains glycyrrhizic acid that is hydrolyzed to glycyrrhetinic acid after ingestion. Glycyrrhetinic acid inhibits 11β-hydroxysteroid dehydrogenase type II and enzymes that reversibly catalyze the conversion of cortisol to cortisone in the distal renal tubule. This intrarenal metabolism of cortisol protects the mineralocorticoid receptor from cortisol. The inhibition of this enzyme by glycyrrhetinic acid raises intrarenal cortisol levels, providing free access to the mineralocorticoid receptor. Accordingly, cortisol is the offending mineralocorticoid in these patients and a low plasma renin activity and low aldosterone concentration is expected. Therefore, her intermittent licorice use (Answer C) is not the cause of her hypertension.

##### Educational Objective:

Identify the increases in aldosterone and cortisol due to steroid receptor blockade and changes in the steroid-binding protein concentration.

63 ANSWER: E) No thyroid hormone replacement

This patient has a serum TSH level that is above the laboratory reference range (0.5-5.0 mIU/L). Generally, such reference ranges are reported for healthy populations without evidence of thyroid disease and are not further adjusted for other characteristics. It is now appreciated that many other factors, including age, sex, ethnicity, and the pregnant state, can alter the reference range for serum TSH. Particularly notable are the effects of age and pregnancy. With respect to age, both the median serum TSH concentration and the 97.5th percentile increase with advancing age. Such data have led to recommendations that both levothyroxine initiation and titration of levothyroxine therapy be considered in the context of relevant patient characteristics.

#### ANSWER: E) Refer to a bariatric surgeon for consultation

64 This patient with polycystic ovary syndrome has prediabetes on the basis of her elevated fasting blood glucose and hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ levels. Interventions to reduce the risk of progression to frank diabetes are reasonable in such patients. On the basis of results of several clinical trials, including the US-based Diabetes Prevention Program, significant lifestyle modification can clearly be effective in reducing this risk. Lifestyle modification counseling, which includes goals of 7% weight loss and physical activity for at least 150 minutes per week, is recommended for all patients with prediabetes. Implementation of such changes reduces the rate of

Although no medications are specifically approved for this purpose, pharmacotherapy with antihyperglycemic agents or other medications may also be considered to reduce the risk of diabetes in this population. The Diabetes Prevention Program has demonstrated that metformin therapy, although less effective overall than lifestyle modification, reduces the conversion from prediabetes to diabetes by approximately 30%. Interestingly, metformin appears to provide a greater benefit in patients younger than 60 years, patients with a BMI greater than 35 kg/m², and in women with a history of gestational diabetes. Groups including the American Diabetes Association endorse consideration of metformin therapy for individuals at high risk for progression to diabetes, including very obese individuals and those with clinically significant and/or progressive hyperglycemia.

The patient in this case has adopted lifestyle modification and been prescribed metformin therapy; however, she has lost only 5% of her body weight and has had no further recent weight loss. In addition, given her available hemoglobin  $ A_{1c} $ values, her hyperglycemia appears to be progressive. Reinforcement of lifestyle change is appropriate in this situation, but it is also reasonable to reassess her current management and determine whether other interventions may be beneficial.

Although other types of medications have demonstrated efficacy in reducing the risk of diabetes, their role in the care of individuals such as this patient is less clear. Acarbose, an  $ \alpha $-glucosidase inhibitor approved for the treatment of diabetes, reduces the relative risk of diabetes by 25% in patients with impaired glucose tolerance. However, substitution of an  $ \alpha $-glucosidase inhibitor for the patient's metformin therapy (Answer A) would not be considered more effective in reducing her diabetes risk than her current regimen. In clinical trials, thiazolidinediones including rosiglitazone and pioglitazone reduce the risk of diabetes by 60% to 70% in individuals at high risk. Unfortunately, such therapy is associated with weight gain, fluid retention, and an increased risk of congestive heart failure and fractures. Given these concerns and this patient's very significant baseline obesity, substitution of a thiazolidinedione for this patient's metformin therapy (Answer B) is not the best next step. Patients with prediabetes are considered to be at increased cardiovascular risk and should be evaluated for contributory conditions such as hypertension and dyslipidemia. This patient's blood pressure of 135/78 mm Hg, although not normal, does not currently warrant pharmacologic intervention. Initiation of an ACE inhibitor (Answer C) is not indicated now for the treatment of hypertension, and it has not been shown to reduce the risk of cardiovascular events in a population at high risk for diabetes. In patients with established diabetes, the American Diabetes Association now recommends initiation of pharmacologic antihypertensive therapy if the systolic blood pressure is  $ \geq $140 mm Hg and/or the diastolic blood pressure is  $ \geq $80 mm Hg.

Medical societies including the American Association of Clinical Endocrinologists have emphasized weight loss as the primary strategy to reduce the risk of progression from prediabetes to diabetes. Weight loss may be achieved through the adoption of essentially any dietary modification that includes an energy deficit through reduced caloric intake. There is no evidence that any particular dietary composition yields a significant sustained weight loss advantage compared with others; thus, recommending that this patient change to a low-carbohydrate diet (Answer D) would not be beneficial.

Referral to a bariatric surgeon for consultation (Answer E) is an appropriate recommendation in this particular clinical situation. Bariatric surgery is highly effective in preventing progression to diabetes, with the effectiveness largely dependent on the amount of weight loss achieved. This patient has class III obesity with the comorbid condition of prediabetes. Despite appropriate lifestyle counseling, she has had progressive hyperglycemia. Recently published guidelines for the management of overweight and obesity issued by the American Heart Association, American College of Cardiology, and The Obesity Society endorse such a referral as an adjunct to comprehensive lifestyle intervention in those with a BMI of 40 kg/m² or higher or those with a BMI of 35 kg/m² higher and obesity-related comorbidity.

##### Educational Objective:

Recommend bariatric surgery as a management option for a morbidly obese patient with prediabetes.
