To: Endo doctor [endodoctor@healthcare.com]

Subject: Re: interesting patient question

Dear Endo Doc,

Thanks for asking me about your patient. I appreciate your confidence. The presence of a very low serum cortisol and plasma ACTH suggest that the patient has secondary endogenous adrenal insufficiency. The elevated plasma renin activity and low aldosterone can be attributed to the use of an ACE inhibitor. In the absence of any evidence of primary adrenal failure, there is no need for adrenal CT (Answer B). Since glucocorticoids such as cortisol exert negative feedback on vasopressin, hyponatremia is common in patients with secondary adrenal insufficiency. Your patient has clinical features consistent with excessive glucocorticoid exposure with facial rounding, increased dorsocervical fat, accelerated hypertension, and cutaneous wasting. In the presence of these findings, the low ACTH and cortisol are probably caused by suppression of hypothalamic corticotropin-releasing hormone and pituitary ACTH by exogenously administered glucocorticoids. Even with periodic or intermittent Cushing disease, the serum cortisol levels and ACTH levels are not fully suppressed during the “nonCushing” part of the cycle. The normal brain MRI certainly excludes any clinically significant pituitary lesion that might cause pituitary hypoadrenalism, and a dedicated pituitary MRI (Answer C) would not be helpful in this patient.

Exogenous corticosteroid exposure is the only cause of both Cushing syndrome and secondary endogenous adrenal hypofunction. Unfortunately, many patients receiving steroid injections have no idea that they are receiving corticosteroids that may affect their metabolic milieu and physiology. For example, epidural steroid administration (especially when multiple injections are given over a few weeks) suppresses the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis and occasionally causes signs and symptoms of Cushing syndrome. Intra-articular steroids may have a similar fate and plasma levels of these exogenously administered steroids may be detectable in the plasma for many weeks after administration, as they are slowly absorbed from a closed space. Factualious Cushing syndrome is a rare disorder that implies surreptitious intake of a glucocorticoid. However, occasionally patients may purchase “vitamins” from outside the United States for arthritis and other ailments. Some of these preparations contain large amounts of corticosteroids—initially the patient feels much better, but after a few months, the patient develops Cushing syndrome.

A synthetic screen for glucocorticoids (Answer D) is available. Using liquid chromatography-tandem mass spectrometry, many commonly administered steroids can be quantified. I am certain this would provide you with the reason your patient has low cortisol and ACTH levels.

Unlike the precursor for aldosterone (11-deoxycorticosterone) that has agonist activity at the mineralocorticoid receptor, 11-deoxycortisol (precursor for cortisol) (Answer E) has no agonist activity at the glucocorticoid receptor. Although a mutation in the CYP11B1 gene (11β-hydroxylase) causes the second most common type of congenital adrenal hyperplasia, you would expect this disorder to present with ambiguous genitalia in female patients (penile enlargement in boys) with hypokalemia and hypertension developing later. However, a late-onset form of congenital adrenal hyperplasia is very rare. Of course, in any form of congenital adrenal hyperplasia, you would not expect a suppressed plasma ACTH and clinical Cushing syndrome. Carriers of a CYP11B1 mutation have no detectable clinical or biochemical abnormalities. Measurement of serum 11-deoxycortisol would not be useful; it will be undetectable in this woman.

The major binding protein for cortisol also referred to as serpin A6, belongs to the major binding protein for cortisol.

Corticosteroid-binding globulin (CBG, transcortin), also referred to as serpin A6, belongs to the serpin superfamily and it is the major binding protein for cortisol. CBG is a 52-kDa secreted  $ \alpha1 $-glycoprotein consisting of 405 amino acids. It is synthesized and secreted by hepatocytes in the liver and is present in glycocorticoid responsive cells. The concentration of CBG is regulated by estrogens. CBG is the main transport protein for progestins and glucocorticoids in the blood. CBG levels increase in states of increased estrogen secretion or administration and levels may be very low in critically ill patients with hypoproteinemia. Defects in the gene encoding CBG are the cause of CBG deficiency, a rare disorder characterized by reduced CBG production that results in hypotension or hypertension and muscle fatigue. Because you measured a very low salivary cortisol level in the morning (clever idea), this confirms true endogenous cortisol deficiency and excludes a binding protein abnormality. As you know, salivary cortisol is an easily accessible surrogate of “free” biologically active cortisol that represents approximately 5% total cortisol. The concentration of unbound or free cortisol can also be calculated from the concentration of total cortisol and that of CBG on the basis of mass action. Nonetheless, measuring CBG (Answer A) would not be helpful in this patient.

Finally, she does not need to be treated with hydrocortisone replacement since the exogenously administered steroid should provide adequate glucocorticoid support until the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis recovers. Nonetheless, it might be prudent to use stress steroid dosing for a serious illness or stressful event.

I hope this has been helpful. Please let me know what happens.

Dr. Compound F

Establishing the cause of Cushing's syndrome

#### Reference(s):

109 ANSWER: E) Erythrocytosis

To achieve masculinization of a female-to-male transgender individual, testosterone is administered to target a normal male range of 300 to 1200 ng/dL (10.4 to 41.6 nmol/L), 1 week after testosterone administration if given intramuscularly, but levels less than 700 ng/dL (24.3 nmol/L) are recommended. Transgender individuals should be seen every 3 months during the first year of treatment, then every 6 to 12 months to monitor testosterone levels; the effectiveness of therapy; and potential adverse effects including erythrocytosis, weight gain, salt retention, lipid abnormalities, cystic acne, and psychological changes. The testosterone level in this individual is greater than 700 ng/dL (24.3 nmol/L) the day before administration of the next testosterone dose, suggesting that his testosterone levels are always higher than the recommended range. Testosterone replacement may cause erythrocytosis (Answer E). The transgender man in this vignette is also overweight and has acne, which might improve slightly with a lower testosterone dosage.

Few transgender men can afford to have a hysterectomy, salpingo-oophorectomy, and mastectomy. Therefore, clinical breast examinations, mammograms, and Papanicolaou testing should be performed at recommended intervals as indicated by the individual's risk group. Testosterone levels in the male range often result in amenorrhea. Female-to-male transgender patients are at risk for endometrial cancer based on aromatization of testosterone to estradiol (Answer D), and endometrial hyperplasia was described in 3 of 19 transgender patients treated with androgens. However, the endometrium is typically atrophic with high-dosage testosterone therapy when examined after hysterectomy and no cases of endometrial cancer have been reported. Therefore, the actual risk of endometrial cancer is unknown.

Liver function abnormalities (Answer A) should not increase with intramuscular testosterone administration, although liver function should be evaluated every 6 months to 1 year because transient elevation in liver enzymes is common.

Low bone mineral density (Answer C) is not a concern if testosterone is maintained in the male range.

Although male-to-female transgender individuals on estrogen therapy are at high risk for thromboembolic events (Answer B), female-to-male transgender individuals are not.

##### Educational Objective:

Monitor hormone levels and potential adverse effects of testosterone therapy in female-to-male transgender patients.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Treatment of transsexualism

##### Reference(s):

Committee on Health Care for Underserved Women. Committee Opinion no. 512: health care for transgender individuals. Obstet Gynecol. 2011;118(6):1454-1458.

Hembree WC, Cohen-Kettenis P, Delemarre-van de Waal HA, et al; Endocrine Society. Endocrine treatment of transsexual persons: an Endocrine Society clinical practice guideline. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2009;94(9):3132-3154.

Futterweit W, Deligdisch L. Histopathological effects of exogenously administered testosterone in 19 female-to-male transsexuals. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 1986;62(1):16-21.

## 110 ANSWER: E) Measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number

Although laboratory and clinical parameter-based assessments of cardiovascular risk have been validated in a wide range of populations, they are not perfect. Individuals who have low predicted risk might still experience a cardiovascular event; conversely, individuals who have high predicted risk may never have an event, even when risk-reducing therapies (such as lipid-lowering therapies) are not used. Thus, there is an ongoing interest in additional means to assess cardiovascular risk, especially in situations such as the one described in this vignette—this patient scores at “intermediate” risk using the Framingham risk calculator.

Apolipoprotein B (Answer E) is the main structural protein for atherogenic lipoproteins (VLDL, IDL, LDL), but it is not present in HDL. There is 1 apolipoprotein B molecule per lipoprotein particle, so apolipoprotein B indicates particle number, whereas the LDL cholesterol indicates the amount of cholesterol found in the LDL fraction. An LDL-cholesterol concentration of 100 mg/dL (2.59 mmol/L) could be due to many smaller particles (with less cholesterol per particle) or fewer larger particles (with proportionately more cholesterol per particle). Because the smaller, denser LDL particles are thought to be more atherogenic than larger, more buoyant particles.

there has been an emerging interest in measuring particle number, and several commercial labs provide this service. The INTERHEART study was a large international study of cardiovascular risk in which apolipoprotein B levels were found to be a significant predictor of myocardial infarction, even after adjusting for age and traditional lipid risk factors. However, other studies have refuted this finding, so it remains controversial. There is potential redundancy because measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number can be approximated by the use of the non-HDL-cholesterol concentration: if the non-HDL-cholesterol level is more than 30 mg/dL (0.78 mmol/L) above the LDL-cholesterol level, this indicates high particle number. Some guidelines have recommended measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number, particularly in patients at high risk, to enhance risk assessment and provide apolipoprotein B goals for therapy. Although routine measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number has not been uniformly adopted, there is some suggestion in the literature that determining these values may be appropriate in individuals at very high risk, in those with hypertriglyceridemia, or in those with genetic dyslipidemias. Although cost-effectiveness for mass screening has not been formally assessed for apolipoprotein B measurement, it is relatively low-cost assay and is well validated in several populations. In this patient with elevated triglycerides, it may more accurately assess risk than measurement of LDL cholesterol or non-HDL cholesterol, and it is the best choice of the options presented here. However, it is important to note that measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number is not presently part of most guidelines. In fact, recent studies suggest that up to 75% of cholesterol laboratory testing could be avoided in a cost-effective strategy of cardiovascular risk assessment via a multistage screening approach using non-laboratory risk factors such as age, smoking status, blood pressure, and BMI.

The coronary artery calcium (CAC) score (Answer A) is determined by a CT-based scan that detects calcium, which indicates atherosclerosis in asymptomatic individuals. It has been proposed as a tool to detect disease (and thus risk for cardiovascular events) in persons who may be incorrectly assessed as being at low risk by traditional risk factors such as the Framingham score. Meta-analyses of CAC studies have found correlations between CAC scores and cardiac outcomes, with a relative risk of a cardiac event of 4.4 if CAC is present vs absent. As the CAC score increases, so does the risk of cardiac events. Furthermore, the CAC score varies between individuals even if they have the same or similar Framingham risk scores. However, the CAC score itself is not perfect because it only detects calcified plaque, and many dangerous (at risk of rupturing) atherosclerotic plaque lesions are not yet calcified; thus, the CAC score can underestimate disease. Conversely, CAC scores can overestimate risk in calcified low-risk-for-rupturing lesions. Cost-effectiveness analyses of CAC scoring have not supported its use, and there are health concerns with the procedure itself because of radiation exposure. Therefore, CAC scoring is not routinely recommended, nor is it cost-effective. There is no evidence that knowledge of CAC score improves adherence to lifestyle measures.

HDL has been long regarded as the protective lipid particle, and epidemiologic studies demonstrate that a low HDL-cholesterol concentration is associated with increased cardiovascular events. Conversely, higher HDL-cholesterol levels are thought to be protective. However, recent studies attempting to raise HDL cholesterol using fibrates (ACCORD [Action to Control Cardiovascular Risk in Diabetes]) or niacin (AIM-HIGH [Atherosclerosis Intervention in Metabolic Syndrome with Low HDL/High Triglyceride and Impact on Global Health Outcomes]) in statin-treated patients have not shown any additional benefit in cardiovascular outcomes, thus casting doubt on the utility of HDL-cholesterol measurement. There is an emerging interest in assessment of HDL function (Answer B), which includes antioxidant, anti-inflammatory, and reverse cholesterol transport activities. However, to date these assessments are available in research laboratories only, not in clinical practice.

Coronary CT angiography (CCTA) (Answer C) is a newer tool using CT imaging to assess the presence of coronary artery disease. A recent study evaluated outcomes in more than 24,000 patients without known cardiovascular disease and determined that the absence of coronary disease was associated with a favorable prognosis. The study also documented a correlation between the number of coronary vessels with obstructive coronary artery disease and mortality. Thus, CCTA is emerging as a new tool to diagnose coronary artery disease and stratify risk in persons with detected coronary artery disease. Findings from additional studies comparing CCTA with invasive coronary angiography in patients with coronary symptoms have suggested that CCTA may have better utility. At this time, there are no cost-effectiveness data to support CCTA screening in primary prevention.

Lipoprotein(a) (Answer D) is an atherogenic lipoprotein not measured in most routine lipid panels.

to be an independent risk factor for cardiovascular disease, although the relative risk ratio for an elevated

lipoprotein(a) level is modest. The association between lipoprotein(a) and cardiovascular events appears to be

more related to certain subtypes of lipoprotein(a) rather than simply the lipoprotein(a) levels, and to date no evidence has documented that lowering lipoprotein(a) is beneficial (niacin is the only lipid-lowering agent that reduces lipoprotein[a]). Thus, lipoprotein(a) measurement is not recommended for routine screening. Most experts recommend measurement of lipoprotein(a) only if there is a strong family history of premature cardiovascular disease, particularly if there is no obvious dyslipidemia to explain such a family history.

Assessing the costs of various tests is difficult because costs range widely among providers, laboratories, and countries. As rough estimates, a coronary artery calcium scan costs between $150 and $500 (US dollars); contrast-enhanced coronary CT angiography costs between $500 and $1500; measurement of lipoprotein(a) costs between $50 and $250; and measurement of apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number costs between $50 and $200 (the costs for lipoprotein[a] and/or apolipoprotein B or LDL particle number often include the cost of the standard lipid panel).

##### Educational Objective:

Evaluate the utility of tools available to estimate cardiovascular risk beyond life factor— and lipid panel-based risk assessment.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Overview of the risk equivalents and established risk factors for cardiovascular disease

##### Reference(s):

Pandya A, Weinstein MC, Salomon JA, Cutler D, Gaziano TA. Who needs laboratories and who needs statins?: comparative and cost-effectiveness analyses of non-laboratory-based, laboratory-based, and staged primary cardiovascular disease screening guidelines. Circ Cardiovasc Qual Outcomes. 2014;7(1):25-32.

Waugh N, Black C, Walker S, McIntyre L, Cummins E, Hillis G. The effectiveness and cost-effectiveness of computed tomography screening for coronary artery disease disease: systematic review. Health Technol Assess. 2006;10(39):iii-iv, ix-x, 1-41.

Min JK, Dunning A, Lin FY, et al; CONFIRM Investigators. Age- and sex-related differences in all-cause mortality risk based on coronary computed tomography angiography findings results from the International Multicenter CONFIRM (Coronary CT Angiography Evaluation for Clinical Outcomes: An International Multicenter Registry) of 23,854 patients without known coronary artery disease. J Am Coll Cardiol. 2011;58(8):849-860.

Mangalmuri SS, Davidson MH. The incremental value of lipids and inflammatory biomarkers in determining residual cardiovascular risk. Curr Atheroscler Rep. 2011;13(5):373-380.

Sniderman AD, Williams K, Contois JH, Monroe HM, McQueen MJ, de Graaf J, Furberg CD. A meta-analysis of low-density lipoprotein cholesterol, non-density lipoprotein cholesterol, and apolipoprotein B as markers of cardiovascular risk. Circ Cardiovasc Qual Outcomes. 2011;4(3):337-45.

## 111 ANSWER: E) Discontinue risedronate and start denosumab

Bisphosphonates are poorly absorbed orally, with less than 1% absorbed in the gastrointestinal tract. In addition, gastrointestinal adverse effects, including gastritis and esophagitis are relatively common. Often, these symptoms resolve within a few weeks or within a few doses. However, an alternative medication should be prescribed when gastrointestinal symptoms persist. Therefore, continuing the current medication (Answer A) is incorrect. Prescribing another oral bisphosphonate (Answer C) with a similar adverse effect profile when the patient has developed gastrointestinal adverse effects is not a tenable long-term solution. Moreover, ibandronate does not offer fracture protection at the hip. In this patient, intravenous zoledronic acid (Answer D) is desirable because in a trial of zoledronic acid for secondary hip fracture prevention, a mortality benefit was documented and the intravenous administration of the agent solves the issue of gastrointestinal intolerance; however, this patient's reduced renal function is a contraindication to use of zoledronic acid.

Given his history of hip fracture and low bone density in the osteoporotic range, he should receive treatment. Alternative therapies include calcitonin, teriparatide, or denosumab. In this patient, replacing risedronate with calcitonin (Answer B), which does not offer fracture protection at the hip, is not a good choice. Teriparatide (not an option offered in this vignette) is indicated to increase bone mass in men with primary or hypogonadal osteoporosis at high risk for fracture. Thus, it would be an option for this man. Denosumab (Answer E) given by injection once every 6 months, reduces the risk of fracture at both the hip and spine, as demonstrated in the FREEDOM trial. Because it is given by subcutaneous injection, it avoids the gastrointestinal adverse effects common to bisphosphonates and it can be used in the setting of chronic kidney disease.

### Educational Objective:

Educational Objectives: Prioritize the choice of osteoporosis therapy on the basis of patient characteristics and contraindications.

ToDate Topic Review(s):

Treatment of osteoporosis in men

#### Reference(s):

Cummings SR, San Martin J, McClung MR, et al; FREEDOM Trial. Denosumab for prevention of fractures in postmenopausal women with osteoporosis published correction in N Engl J Med. 2009;361(19):1914]. N Engl J Med. 2009;361(8):756-765.

#### Answer: E) Recommend no additional therapy at this time

112 Answer: Sodium-glucose cotransporter 2 (SGLT2) inhibitors constitute a newer category of diabetes therapy. Their use is currently indicated either in conjunction with lifestyle modification as monotherapy or with other medications. SGLT2 inhibitors lower blood glucose by decreasing SGLT2 activity in the proximal renal tubule. They lower the renal threshold for glucose and thereby increase glucose excretion in the urine and lower the plasma glucose concentration. This may cause polyuria and glucosuria. Canagliflozin should be taken before the initial meal of the day and can be started at a dosage of 100 mg daily and then increased to a maximum dosage of 300 mg daily, depending on normal renal function.

300 mg daily, up to 300 mg taking SGLT2 inhibitors can develop urinary tract infections related to glycosuria (approximately 5% incidence). This may be associated with symptoms of dysuria and polyuria. Urine analysis, as ordered in this vignette, helps to diagnose this adverse reaction, which should be treated with antibiotic therapy once confirmed. This patient's urine analysis documented that she does have a urinary tract infection. Because of SGLT2 inhibitors' mechanism of action, treated patients are also at an increased risk of developing vulvovaginal candidiasis, vulvovaginal mycotic infection, vulvovaginitis, and vulvitis (approximately 10% incidence). Men are at an increased risk of developing balanitis or balanopostitis and genital fungal infection. Patients who have a history of such infections or uncircumcised men are at an increased risk of developing these complications. Women usually report increased vaginal itching or discharge. Similar symptoms can be present in men. These symptoms are attributed to the medication and are easily treated with antifungal therapy such as fluconazole.

Occurrence of a single urinary tract infection or instance of genital mycosis does not necessitate discontinuing or reducing the dosage of SGLT2 inhibitor therapy (thus, Answers B and C are incorrect). Most episodes respond rapidly and do not recur. Thus, recommending no additional therapy now (Answer E) is appropriate.

Pelvic inflammatory disease, such as endometritis, is usually associated with lower abdominal pain that is often bilateral and exacerbated by sexual intercourse. Vaginal discharge, abnormal uterine bleeding, and systemic symptoms such as fever with chills may also occur. Lack of these symptoms makes this a very unlikely diagnosis. Endometritis is not an adverse reaction of SGLT2 inhibitors and the findings on urinalysis suggest a urinary tract infection (thus, Answer D is incorrect).

Intrauterine devices have relatively few adverse effects. In the past, intrauterine devices were linked with pelvic inflammatory disease, most likely because of the construction of braided and multifilament tails. These designs may have allowed bacteria to ascend the upper genital tract and produce infection. Currently available IUDs have monofilament tail strings and pelvic inflammatory disease is no longer a significant risk (thus, Answer A is incorrect).

#### Educational Objective:

Manage the occurrence of a urinary tract infection in the setting of sodium-glucose cotransporter 2 inhibitor therapy.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Management of persistent hyperglycemia in type 2 diabetes mellitus

Musso G, Gambino R, Cassader M, Pagano G. A novel approach to control hyperglycemia in type 2 diabetes: sodium glucose co-transport (SGLT) inhibitors: systematic review and meta-analysis of randomized trials. Ann Med. 2012;44(4):375-393.

Stenlöf K, Cefalu WT, Kim KA, et al. Efficacy and safety of canagliflozin monotherapy in subjects with type 2 diabetes mellitus inadequately controlled with diet and exercise. Diabetes Obes Metab. 2013;15(4):372-382.

## 113 ANSWER: A) Perform FNAB of the palpable thyroid nodule

113 AN Patients presenting with nodular goiter should have a complete history, physical examination, and serum TSH measurement. Although neck palpation is inexpensive, widely available, and simple to perform, it is notoriously imprecise and insensitive in the detection and estimation of size and number of thyroid nodules. All patients with a nodular thyroid should be evaluated with ultrasonography to confirm the presence of a mass, to assess whether the lesion is single or multiple, to determine whether it is of thyroidal origin, and to guide FNAB—the procedure of choice in the evaluation of thyroid nodules. Patients with multiple thyroid nodules have the same risk of malignancy as those with solitary thyroid nodules. Additional nodules are detected in up to 50% of patients presenting with a single palpable nodule when ultrasonography is performed. Selection of those nodules that require biopsy in patients with a multinodular goiter requires careful consideration. Certain ultrasonographic features are associated with increased risk of malignancy, including hypoechogenicity, the presence of microcalcifications, taller than wide shape, increased vascular flow, irregular borders, and absence of a halo. Nodules with any of these features should be aspirated preferentially. The combination of several of these sonographic features increases specificity.

The patient in this vignette has multiple benign-appearing nodules that are well defined, isoechoic, and have no suspicious ultrasonographic features. In this situation, the best recommendation is to aspirate the largest or dominant nodule(s) and to observe the rest with periodic ultrasonography (Answer A). Nodules that have been reported to have benign cytologic characteristics have a false-negative rate of up to 5%; thus, follow-up is recommended within 6 to 18 months of the initial investigation. Although there is no consensus on the definition of clinically significant growth of a thyroid nodule, guidelines from the American Thyroid Association recommend another biopsy when there has been at least a 50% increase in nodule volume within 6 to 18 months from the initial FNAB. If the nodule size is stable, another FNAB is not required and the interval before the next clinical evaluation may be extended to 3 to 5 years.

The American Thyroid Association guidelines provide nodule threshold sizes for FNAB. For mixed cystic-solid nodules such as the ones found in this patient, the recommendation is to aspirate those nodules larger than 2 cm. However, size is not a specific feature to predict risk of malignancy. Since 2 of the 3 mixed cystic-solid nodules in this patient are smaller than 2 cm and have no suspicious features on ultrasonography, performing FNAB on all 3 nodules (Answer B) is incorrect. Notably, the recent guidelines from the British Thyroid Association do not use nodule size as a criterion for FNAB in thyroid nodules. The American Thyroid Association guidelines are currently under revision.

Serum thyroglobulin can be elevated in most thyroid disease and its measurement is a nonsensitive and nonspecific test for thyroid cancer. Although the serum thyroglobulin concentration is useful to monitor patients for residual or recurrent disease after treatment for thyroid cancer, it is not recommended in the initial evaluation of thyroid nodules (thus, Answer C is incorrect). Thyroglobulin antibodies occur in approximately 25% of patients with thyroid cancer and in 10% of the general population.

When present, they may falsely lower serum thyroglobulin concentrations in immunometric assays. Thyroglobulin antibodies should be quantitatively assessed with every thyroglobulin measurement.

Although it is important to follow-up the aspirated and nonaspirated nodules with ultrasonography, it is advisable to aspirate the largest dominant nodule. Ultrasonographic follow-up without aspiration of the largest nodule (Answer D) is not the most appropriate course of action.

In patients with nodular goiter and suppressed serum TSH concentrations, thyroid scanning with either technetium 99m pertechnetate or  $ {}^{123} $I should be performed to assess the functional status of thyroid nodules and to determine the presence of autonomous thyroid nodules. Thyroid scintigraphy (Answer E) has low diagnostic accuracy in the prediction of malignancy in thyroid nodules and is not recommended routinely in patients with normal serum TSH concentrations.

# Educational Objective: Manage nontoxic nodular goiter and select nodules that require fine-needle aspiration biopsy.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Diagnostic approach to and treatment of thyroid nodules

Revised American Thyroid Association management guidelines for patients with thyroid nodules and differentiated thyroid cancer [published corrections appear in Thyroid. 2010;20(8):942 and Thyroid. 2010;20(6):674-675]. Thyroid. 2009;19(11):1167-1214.

Moon WJ, Baek JH, Jung SL, et al. Ultrasonography and the ultrasound-based management of thyroid nodules: consensus statement and recommendations. Korean J Radiol. 2011;12(1):1-14.

Shrestha M, Crothers BA, Burch HB. The impact of thyroid nodule size on the risk of malignancy and accuracy of fine-needle aspiration: a 10-year study from a single institution. Thyroid. 2012;22(12):1251-1256.

Perros P, Boelaert K, Colley S, et al. Guidelines for the management of thyroid cancer. Clin Endocrinol (Oxf). 2014;81(Suppl 1):1-122.

## 114 ANSWER: B) 1.5 L of isotonic saline over the next hour, and intravenous insulin infusion at 12 units per hour

This older patient with type 2 diabetes has developed clinically significant hyperglycemia and an altered mental status requiring hospitalization. She meets established criteria for the hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state (HHS) because she has severe hyperglycemia (plasma glucose >600 mg/dL [>33.30 mmol/L]), hyperosmolality (effective serum osmolality >320 mOsm/kg [>320 mmol/kg]), and dehydration without evidence of marked ketoacidosis. In HHS, the arterial pH is typically above 7.30, the serum bicarbonate is greater than 15 mEq/L (>15 mmol/L), and the anion gap is variable. If detected, only small amounts of ketones are present in the urine and serum. This differs from diabetic ketoacidosis in which hyperglycemia is associated with elevated serum and urinary ketones and metabolic acidosis. In contrast to what is observed in diabetic ketoacidosis, endogenous insulin levels in HHS are generally high enough to prevent lipolysis and the generation of ketone bodies but are not high enough to facilitate glucose uptake into insulin-sensitive tissues.

HHS is estimated to comprise approximately 1% of all diabetes-related hospital admissions, and it is most commonly precipitated by inadequate or omitted insulin dosing or an infectious process. However, HHS may also be associated with conditions such as cerebrovascular or cardiovascular events, pancreatitis, or the use of medications such as glucocorticoids or thiazide diuretics. Older persons with type 2 diabetes are at greatest risk for the development of HHS because they are more likely to become severely dehydrated due to immobilization or an impaired thirst mechanism. The identification and appropriate management of HHS is essential. Even in developed countries, the associated mortality rate is estimated to be between 5% and 20% and is largely attributable to underlying precipitating conditions.

Individuals with HHS are very dehydrated, with estimated total water deficits at presentation typically between 8 and 10 L. Fluid repletion alone will greatly reduce hyperglycemia and reduce insulin resistance, and it represents a critical component of care. As is the case with this patient, the measured serum sodium is often low; however, this is misleading because it is due to the hyperglycemia-induced osmotic flux of water from the intracellular space to the extracellular space. This patient is in fact hypernatremic when the measured sodium level is corrected for the plasma glucose. Thus, the administration of hypertonic saline (Answer A) is not indicated and, in fact, might worsen her hyperosmolarity and hypernatremia. Initial fluid therapy in HHS should include isotonic saline administered at a rate of 15 to 20 mL/kg (or 1 to 1.5 L) over the first hour. Thereafter, hyponatremic patients should be administered isotonic saline at a rate of 250 to 500 mL/h, while patients with normal to high sodium levels should receive 0.45% saline at that rate. Administration of 0.45% saline at this time (Answers D and E) does not represent appropriate initial fluid resuscitation. Although not highlighted in this case, patients with HHS often have an initially high potassium level due to the shift of potassium into the extracellular space but are in fact total-body potassium depleted. Potassium replacement should begin when the serum potassium level falls below the upper limit of the normal range and should generally be maintained between 4 and 5 mEq/L (4 and 5 mmol/L).

HHS should also be managed via a low-dosage insulin infusion protocol. Administration of an initial 0.1 unit/kg body weight intravenous insulin bolus followed by 0.1 unit/kg per hour continuous insulin infusion is recommended; alternatively, administration of a continuous intravenous insulin infusion at a rate of 0.14 unit/kg body weight per hour is also appropriate. Use of such low-dosage insulin protocols generally decreases plasma glucose by 50 to 75 mg/dL per hour (2.78 to 4.16 mmol/L). Administration of isotonic saline now and an intravenous insulin infusion at 12 units per hour (Answer B) would adequately address this patient's immediate requirements. An intravenous insulin infusion of 4 units per hour (Answers C and E) would most likely not result in adequate glucose lowering. The primary goal of glycemic therapy is the maintenance of plasma glucose between 250 and 300 mg/dL (13.88 and 16.65 mmol/L) until the HHS is resolved. This is accomplished through adjustment of the rate of insulin infusion and the administration of dextrose. When the plasma glucose concentration reaches 300 mg/dL (16.65 mmol/L), the intravenous insulin infusion rate should be reduced to 0.02 to 0.05 units/kg per hour and dextrose should be added to the intravenous fluids. HHS is considered resolved when serum osmolality and mental status have returned to normal.

Educational Objective:

Manage the hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Diabetic ketoacidosis and hyperosmolar hyperglycemic state in adults: Treatment

Reference(s):

Steenkamp DW, Alexanian SM, McDonnell ME. Adult hyperglycemic crises: a review and perspective. Curr Diab Rep. 2013;13(1):130-137.

Nyenwe EA, Kitabchi AE. Evidence-based management of hyperglycemic emergencies in diabetes mellitus. Diabetes Res Clin Pract. 2011;94(3):340-351.

Kitabchi AE, Umpierrez GE, Miles JM, Fisher JN. Hyperglycemic crises in adult patients with diabetes. Diabetes Care. 2009;32(7):1335-1343.

## 115 ANSWER: D) Buprenorphine

115 Chronic nonmalignant pain lasting longer than 6 months affects 15% to 30% of the population. Most of these patients benefit from combined treatment with physical modalities (warm compresses, acupuncture) and nonopioid analgesics (mainly nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory agents). However, approximately 20% to 25% of these patients do not benefit from traditional measures and require treatment with opioid analgesics. The last decade has seen a significant increase in the prescription of opioid analgesics for the treatment of noncancer and cancer-related chronic pain. As a result, physicians are increasingly encountering patients experiencing adverse effects of opioid therapy, including endocrine adverse effects. Although perturbations in adrenal, thyroid, and somatotropic axes have been reported, opioid-induced testosterone deficiency is the most commonly encountered endocrine adverse effect. Studies have shown that men taking opioid analgesics have testosterone levels that are half as high as testosterone levels of their age-matched counterparts taking nonopioid analgesics. This suppressive effect of opioid analgesics on gonadal hormones is seen regardless of their route of administration. Low testosterone levels are observed in 86% of men receiving intrathecal opioids, 89% receiving oral opioids, and 80% receiving transdermal opioids. In some patients, testosterone levels even decline into the castrate range (<50 ng/dL [<1.74 nmol/L]). Affected patients generally present with decreased libido, erectile dysfunction, and fatigue. In addition to sexual dysfunction, these patients also have a high prevalence of osteopenia and osteoporosis. As expected, men with opioid addiction experience similar hormonal perturbations as do patients on prescription opioid analgesics.

Opioid analgesics suppress testosterone levels by inhibiting the synthesis and secretion of GnRH; thus, most patients present with a hormonal pattern consistent with secondary hypogonadism (low or inappropriately normal gonadotropins with low serum testosterone levels). Evidence suggests that this suppression of GnRH synthesis is mediated via the  $ \mu $-opioid receptor. Hence, opioid analgesics that are agonists at the  $ \mu $-opioid receptor suppress the gonadal axis. These include natural derivatives of opium such as morphine (Answer E), semisynthetic opioid analgesics such as oxycodone (Answer B) and fentanyl (Answer C), and synthetic opioid analgesics such as methadone (Answer A). Hence, all of the above-mentioned medications have the potential to suppress serum testosterone levels in this patient and are therefore not the best choices.

Buprenorphine (Answer D) does not suppress the gonadal axis because of its unique pharmacologic profile. It is a partial agonist at the  $ \mu $-opioid receptor and an antagonist at the  $ \kappa $-opioid receptor. These differing effects at the

p- and x-opioid receptors are posited to be the mechanism behind its sparing of the gonadal axis. Buprenorphine is a potent opioid analgesic that was originally approved in the treatment of opioid dependence; however, it is used in the treatment of both noncancer and cancer-related chronic pain. In a study of men with opioid dependence, serum testosterone levels were normal in patients treated with buprenorphine and were comparable to levels in age-matched control participants (Figure 1). Methadone-treated patients had significantly lower levels of serum testosterone. As a result, both libido and erectile function were normal in most men on buprenorphine compared with abnormal function in those on methadone (Figure 2). The patient in this vignette experienced a substantial improvement in his back pain with buprenorphine, while his testosterone levels remained within the normal range.

Bliesener N, Albrecht S, Schwager A, Weckbecker K, Lichtermann D, Klingmüller D. Plasma testosterone and sexual function in men receiving buprenorphine maintenance for opioid dependence. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2005;90(1):203-206.

Hallinan R, Byrne A, Agho K, McMahon C, Tynan P, Attia J. Erectile dysfunction in men receiving methadone and buprenorphine maintenance treatment. J Sex Med. 2008;5(3):684-692.

Vuong C, Van Uum SH, O'Dell LE, Lutfy K, Friedman TC. The effects of opioids and opioid analogs on animal and human endocrine systems. Endocr Rev. 2010;31(1):98-132

Pergolizzi J, Aloisi AM, Dahan A, et al. Current knowledge of buprenorphine and its unique pharmacological profile. Pain Pract. 2010;10(5):428-450.

116 ANSWER: C) Decrease vitamin D intake to 800 IU daily. Maintaining adequate calcium and vitamin D intake is a cornerstone in the management and prevention of osteoporosis. The current recommended intake for calcium and vitamin D for women with postmenopausal osteoporosis is 1200 mg daily and 800 IU daily, respectively. With discontinuation of the additional vitamin D₃ tablet, this patient's intake will be consistent with these dosages (thus, Answer C is correct and Answer E is incorrect). The patient's total daily intake of elemental calcium is 1200 mg, so Answers A and B are incorrect. No studies have clearly determined the optimal intake for calcium and vitamin D in premenopausal women. The current recommendations are 1000 mg daily of calcium and 600 IU daily of vitamin D.

Several over-the-counter vitamin D preparations are available. Although this wealth of choices and access facilitates recommended supplementation, it also increases the risk for toxicity when patients are unknowingly consuming excessive vitamin D. The Institute of Medicine defines the safe upper limit of vitamin D supplementation in healthy adults to be 4000 IU daily. Thus, this patient's current dosage should be reduced. Higher dosages put patients at risk for adverse effects and toxicity, so recognizing when a patient is taking excessive vitamin D supplementation is important. The biologic activity of vitamin  $ D_{3} $ may be slightly greater than that of  $ D_{2} $. Switching to 50,000 IU weekly of vitamin  $ D_{2} $ (Answer D) would most likely result in higher vitamin D levels and is thus not indicated.

Historically, obesity has been considered to be protective against fractures. However, a high prevalence of vitamin D deficiency has been described among obese persons, and this may potentially affect bone health. The beneficial effects of calcium and vitamin D supplementation on bone mineral density and fracture risk remains an active topic of investigation. Findings from several studies support a role for calcium and vitamin D supplementation in protecting against bone loss, but the effect on fracture risk is less clear. Most such studies have been conducted in postmenopausal women older than 50 years.

##### Educational Objective:

Advise patients regarding currently recommended vitamin D supplementation.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Overview of vitamin D

Reference-Intakes-for Chung M, Lee J, Terasawa T, Lau J, Trikalinos TA. Vitamin D with or without calcium supplementation for prevention of cancer and fractures: an updated meta-the U.S. Preventive Services Task Force. Ann Intern Med. 2011;155(12):827-838.

## 117 ANSWER: D) Prednisone The critical element in this

The critical element in this vignette is that the patient does not have elevated PTH levels. His hypercalcemia is secondary to extrarenal conversion to 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D by activated macrophages, rather than primary hyperparathyroidism in which parathyroid autonomy leads to hypercalcemia and elevated PTH. Ectopic activation of vitamin D causes absorptive hypercalcemia and subsequently a suppressed PTH level. Phosphate absorption, like calcium absorption, tends to be increased (and because of hypoparathyroidism, urinary phosphate excretion is reduced), such that the serum calcium-phosphate product is elevated and ectopic calcification and nephrocalcinosis are relatively common.

Pneumocystis jirovecii (formerly Pneumocystis carinii) is an opportunistic infection associated with HIV infection and organ transplant. Hypercalcemia associated with P. jirovecii pneumonia can be severe, and it is due to the extrarenal conversion of 25-hydroxyvitamin D to 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D. The list of granulomatous disorders associated with HIV infection is extensive and includes tuberculosis, mycobacterium avium, histoplasmosis, and P. jirovecii. This patient has multiple manifestations of P. jirovecii pneumonia including fever, dyspnea, and fatigue. In addition, he has tachypnea, low oxygen saturation, and a chest radiograph demonstrating the presence of pneumonia. His CD₄ count is low, and he is not taking antiretroviral agents or P. jirovecii prophylaxis, thus putting him at increased risk for this opportunistic infection. In addition, he has symptomatic hypercalcemia (symptoms include fatigue, anorexia, and constipation).

Appropriate treatment of hypercalcemia in a patient with granulomatous disease and ectopic activation of vitamin D can generally be accomplished by aggressive fluid hydration and administration of glucocorticoids, such as prednisone (thus, Answer D is correct). The use of glucocorticoids is safe and effective in this setting. In addition, once a specific etiology for the granulomatous disease has been confirmed, P. jirovecii in this case, treatment directed at that pathogen (eg, intravenous trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole) should be initiated.

Because the basis for the hypercalcemia is excess absorption of calcium from the gut, which in turn lowers PTH, bone turnover in these individuals is often very low. Subcutaneous calcitonin works to decrease serum calcium levels by inhibiting osteoclastic bone resorption, and to a much lesser extent by increasing renal calcium excretion. Thus, the mechanism of action limits the benefit of calcitonin (Answer E).

Zoledronic acid (Answer B) effectively lowers serum calcium when excessive bone resorption is the cause of hypercalcemia. Similarly, denosumab (Answer C) lowers serum calcium in high bone turnover states, but would not reduce the conversion of 25-hydroxyvitamin D to 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D. Neither agent would therefore be useful in this setting.

Cinacalcet (Answer A) can lower calcium levels in patients with hyperparathyroidism because it sensitizes the parathyroid cells to extracellular calcium. In this patient who has a suppressed PTH level, it would be of no benefit.

##### Educational Objective:

Diagnose hypercalcemia due to granulomatous disease and recommend appropriate treatment.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Hypercalcemia in granulomatous diseases

##### Reference(s):

Thomas CF Jr, Limper AH. Pneumocystis pneumonia. N Engl J Med. 2004;350(24):2487-2498.

Ahmed B, Jaspan JB. Case report: hypercalcemia in a patient with AIDS and Pneumocystis carinii pneumonia. Am J Med Sci. 1993;306(5):313-316.

Lionakis MS, Samonis G, Kontoyiannis DP. Endocrine and metabolic manifestations of invasive fungal infections and systemic antifungal treatment. Mayo Clin Proc. 2008;83(9):1046-1060.

Sistek CJ, Wordell CJ, Hauptman SP. Adjuvant corticosteroid therapy for Pneumocystis carinii pneumonia in AIDS patients. Ann Pharmacother. 1992;26(9):1127-1133.

## 118 ANSWER: D) A second adrenal venous sampling procedure

Primary aldosteronism is very common among persons with drug-resistant hypertension and this is one of the criteria that should prompt screening for this condition. Once primary aldosteronism has been confirmed, further tests are needed to establish whether aldosterone excess is due to bilateral adrenal hyperplasia (idiopathic primary aldosteronism) or an aldosterone-producing adenoma. This distinction is very important because bilateral adrenal hyperplasia should be treated medically with mineralocorticoid antagonists, whereas unilateral aldosterone-producing adenomas can be cured (or at least improved) with unilateral adrenalectomy.

The adrenal CT in this vignette demonstrates a small left adrenal adenoma, which may be an aldosterone-producing adenoma potentially amenable to surgical treatment. However, given the incidence of adrenal incidentaloma in persons older than 40 years, as well as the fact that CT is not sufficiently sensitive to detect adrenal micronodules that may be present in bilateral adrenal hyperplasia, it is recommended that patients older than 40 years undergo further testing with adrenal venous sampling (AVS) to confirm unilateral aldosterone excess before surgery is recommended. This can be done with or without cosyntropin stimulation, as there is conflicting evidence as to whether cosyntropin improves the diagnostic accuracy of AVS. However, it is important to know whether cosyntropin has been used to aid interpretation of AVS as detailed in the following text.

The key to answering the vignette's question is the accurate interpretation of the AVS results; some guidance is outlined:

## 1. Ensure both adrenal veins have been cannulated (Selectivity Index)

Measurement of the ratio of cortisol in the adrenal vein to cortisol in the inferior vena cava calculates the selectivity index. If adrenal vein cannulation is successful, cortisol measurements should be significantly higher here than in the inferior vena cava. A selectivity index cutoff greater than 2 (with cosyntropin) or 3 (without cosyntropin) suggests successful cannulation. Some centers also advocate the measurement of plasma epinephrine in the adrenal veins and peripheral circulation to confirm successful cannulation.

## 2. Determine whether there is unilateral aldosterone excess (Lateralization Index)

To do this, the ratio of aldosterone to cortisol must first be calculated within each adrenal vein. These so-called cortisol-corrected ratios of aldosterone are performed to overcome the potential dilution of aldosterone levels within the adrenal veins by blood flow from the accessory hepatic and inferior phrenic veins. Subsequently, the aldosterone to cortisol ratios between the affected and unaffected side should be calculated (the lateralization index). Current guidelines suggest that a lateralization index greater than 2 without cosyntropin and greater than 4 with cosyntropin is suggestive of lateralized aldosterone excess.

In this vignette, the selectivity index on the right side is 0.9, which suggests that the right adrenal vein was not cannulated. This is a common problem given the small size and awkward positioning of the right adrenal vein. Therefore, although it would appear that the left adrenal is producing excess aldosterone, this is impossible to confirm using these data.

The best course of action under these circumstances would be to recommend another AVS procedure (Answer D) or to simply commence medical therapy. However, the vignette emphasizes that the patient would prefer a surgical cure if possible. Thus, in this situation, repeated AVS is the correct step.

Aldosterone-producing adenomas and benign adrenal adenomas are not fluorodeoxyglucose-PET avid, so a PET-CT scan (Answer A) would not help in securing the correct diagnosis. There has, however, been some evidence that specialized PET-CT scanning using 11C-metomidate tracer may help identify aldosterone-producing adenomas; however, such scans are not currently routinely available and require further evaluation. Bilateral adrenalectomy (Answer C) is never an appropriate treatment of primary aldosteronism, even if there is evidence of bilateral aldosterone excess. Although some evidence in the vignette supports the possibility of a left aldosterone-producing adenoma, the AVS data have not confirmed lateralization of aldosterone excess to the left side. Therefore, to recommend left adrenalectomy (Answer B) on the basis of this information would be wrong, as right-sided aldosterone excess (and hence bilateral adrenal hyperplasia) has not been excluded. If this were bilateral adrenal hyperplasia, left adrenalectomy would not be curative and would expose the patient to an unnecessary and irreversible surgical procedure.

Rarely, aldosterone excess can be caused by an autosomal dominant disorder termed glucocorticoid remediable aldosteronism. It is due to the presence of a chimeric gene incorporating the promoter region of the CYP11B1 gene (11β-hydroxylase) and the remainder of the CYP11B2 gene (aldosterone synthase). Therefore, aldosterone excess develops under control of ACTH, resulting in hypertension. Glucocorticoid remediable aldosteronism is not likely in this vignette because it is far less common than nonfamilial primary aldosteronism and it is not usually associated with adrenal adenomas. Moreover, although dexamethasone suppression testing (to demonstrate ACTH sensitivity of aldosterone excess) (Answer E) was previously the main method of diagnosing this rare condition, current Endocrine Society guidelines recommend genetic testing as the criterion standard diagnostic tool for this condition.

##### Educational Objective:

Interpret the results of adrenal venous sampling in the management of primary aldosteronism.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Approach to the patient with hypertension and hypokalemia

##### Reference(s):

Rossi GP, Auchus RJ, Brown M, et al. An expert consensus statement on use of adrenal vein sampling for the subtyping of primary aldosteronism. Hypertension 2014;63(1):151-160.

Funder JW, Carey RM, Fardella C, et al; Endocrine Society. Case detection, diagnosis, and treatment of patients with primary aldosteronism: an Endocrine Society Clinical Practice Guideline. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2008;93(9):3266-3281.

## 119 ANSWER: B) Tissue transglutaminase antibodies

119 Celiac disease occurs with increased frequency in persons with type 1 diabetes mellitus and in such instances, malabsorption can result in deficiency of iron, folate, and fat-soluble vitamins such as vitamin D. These deficiencies could have adverse effects on maternal and fetal health (especially with respect to folate deficiency and fetal neural tube defects). The presence of microcytosis in a patient with type 1 diabetes and mild anemia should raise the possibility of celiac disease. Measuring tissue transglutaminase antibodies (Answer B) is recognized as an appropriate screening tool in these circumstances and this is the next step for this patient.

On the basis of her physical examination and current laboratory results, measurement of TPO antibodies (Answer A) as a marker of thyroid autoimmunity is unlikely to guide management. She is euthyroid, and with a normal TSH level, the recommendation would be to follow TSH.

Given the patient's unremarkable history, normal physical examination findings, normal serum creatinine concentration, and no microalbuminuria, detailed measures of renal function, such as iothalamate clearance (Answer C), are not indicated.

Because of the patient's age, absence of fracture history, and established diagnosis of diabetes treated with insulin, measurement of bone mineral density (Answer D) or glutamic acid decarboxylase antibodies (Answer E) is unlikely to be helpful in her preconception management. In light of the patient's low 25-hydroxyvitamin D concentration, replacement therapy is indicated.

##### Educational Objective:

Suspect celiac disease in a patient with type 1 diabetes mellitus, microcytosis, and mild anemia.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Pathogenesis, epidemiology, and clinical manifestations of celiac disease in adults

Reference(s):

Schuppan D, Hahn EG. Celiac disease and its link to type 1 diabetes mellitus. J Pediatr Endocrinol Metab. 2001;14(Suppl 1):597-605.

Cronin CC, Feighery A, Ferriss Jb, Liddy C, Shanahan F, Feighery C. High prevalence of celiac disease among patients with insulin-dependent (type I) diabetes mellitus. Am J Gastroenterol. 1997;92(12):2210-2212.

## 120 ANSWER: B) Antidiuretic hormone This patient's pituitary MRI demonstrates

This patient's pituitary MRI demonstrates marked thickening of the pituitary stalk. This is an imaging finding that can be seen in diabetes insipidus due to antidiuretic hormone deficiency (Answer B). Pituitary MRI findings are variable in the context of diabetes insipidus and may be normal or demonstrate an absent posterior pituitary, loss of the posterior pituitary bright spot, or thickening of the pituitary stalk. The latter two features are more often associated with inflammatory disease such as lymphocytic hypophysitis, but they can be found in malignant (metastatic) disease. In a review of 42 adolescents presenting with pituitary stalk thickening on MRI, 16 (38%) also had diabetes insipidus. One-half of those with diabetes insipidus had neoplastic disease, whereas none of the patients with only pituitary stalk thickening (and no diabetes insipidus) had neoplastic disease. In this patient, the pituitary abnormality was due to metastatic disease from her previous breast cancer, and pituitary imaging 6 months later demonstrated a clear seller and suprasellar mass that was confirmed to be metastatic disease.

The pituitary gland is an uncommon site for metastases, as metastatic disease accounts for only 1% to 2% of sellar masses. Although neoplasms from almost every tissue have been reported to metastasize to the pituitary, the

most common primary tumors are breast or lung cancers. Metastatic deposits demonstrate a predilection for the posterior lobe of the pituitary, mainly because there is a direct arterial blood supply (via the inferior hypophyseal artery, which branches from the internal carotid artery) that facilitates hematogenous spread. In a review of more than 200 cases, the posterior lobe was involved alone or with the anterior pituitary in 85% of cases, whereas only the anterior lobe was affected in 15%. Therefore, metastatic deposits in the anterior lobe are usually due to contiguous spread from the posterior lobe.

The patient's imaging findings and previous history of breast cancer make diabetes insipidus the most likely initial endocrine dysfunction in this case. Therefore, all other answers (A, C, D, and E) are incorrect, although they may also develop with time as the metastatic disease progresses and the lesion enlarges. Indeed, in the review of 42 adolescents with pituitary stalk thickening and diabetes insipidus, 44% subsequently developed anterior pituitary dysfunction. In this case, pituitary MRI performed 6 months later demonstrated a clear sellar and suprasellar mass and the patient developed ACTH and TSH deficiencies.

Pituitary metastases are, however, often asymptomatic. In the aforementioned review, 40 of 200 patients (20%) were symptomatic, and, of these, diabetes insipidus was documented in 28 patients (70%) while only 6 patients (15%) had 1 or more anterior pituitary deficiencies.

##### Educational Objective:

Identify the most likely endocrine abnormality associated with pituitary neoplastic disease.

UpToDate Topic Review(s):

Causes, presentation, and evaluation of seller masses

##### Reference(s):

Robison NJ, Prabhu SP, Sun P, et al. Predictors of neoplastic disease in children with isolated pituitary stalk thickening. Pediatr Blood Cancer. 2013;60(10):1630-1635.

Komninos J, Vlassopoulou V, Protopapa D, et al. Tumors metastatic to the pituitary gland: case report and literature review. J Clin Endocrinol Metab. 2004;89(2):574-580.

ESAP™—Endocrine Self-Assessment Program—is an essential continuing medical education resource based on the American Board of Internal Medicine blueprint for certification in endocrinology, diabetes, and metabolism. It is an ideal tool for clinically relevant, self-directed learning for physicians seeking initial certification or recertification in endocrinology, program directors interested in a testing and training instrument, and clinicians simply wanting a self-assessment and a broad review of endocrinology.

Features of ESAP 2015 include:

Completely updated content with 120 new cases

MOC approval from ABIM and RCPSC

Fulfillment of ABIM's new Patient Safety requirement

Online module, hard-copy reference book, conventional and SI units

4Q AMA PRA Category 1 Credits™
